Invitation and Immediate Separation Upon One Spouse's Conversion
Rulings on inviting the non-converting spouse and timing of separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the Hanafi judicial process when one polytheist spouse converts in Dar al-Islam and explain why immediate separation is required, citing the Shafi'i counterargument and the exceptions for minors and insane persons.

Core Idea
Conversion alone does not sever marriage; the judge separates immediately if the other refuses Islam.

When one spouse embraces Islam, the marriage is not automatically dissolved. The judge invites the other; if they refuse, the couple is separated at once. This lesson unpacks the procedure, the Shafi'i opposing view, and how the rules apply to minors and the insane.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger
One of the polytheist spouses converts to Islam while the other remains a polytheist. The marriage is not instantly nullified.
This ruling applies in Dar al-Islam.
2
Outline the judicial procedure
The judge invites the non-converting spouse to embrace Islam. If they refuse (ibā' ), the judge must separate them immediately, whether before or after consummation.
No waiting period is allowed.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i objection
Al-Shafi'i holds that after consummation the separation should be delayed by three menstrual periods. The Hanafi school rejects this, citing the practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab (ra) which was met with silent consensus.
The Hanafi position is based on a companion's action and ijma' sukuti.
4
Apply exceptions for minors and the insane
If the non-converting spouse is a discerning minor, the separation occurs upon refusal like an adult. If the minor is non-discerning, the judge waits until maturity. If the spouse is insane, the judge presents the invitation to the parents because insanity has no fixed end.
The Abu Yusuf disagreement on the minor's refusal is noted but not operative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أسلم: أي الأخر فيها ونعمت وهي امرأته (ط، ع) وإلا فرّق بينهما أي وإن لم يسلم الآخر وأبى الإسلام فرق القاضي بينهما ولا يتوقف، سواء كان الإباء قبل الدخول أو بعده، ففي المجوسيين يفرق بإسلام أحدهما بعد الإباء، وفي الكتابيين إن أسلمت الزوجة يفرق بينهما، وإن أسلم الزوج فلا يتعرض لها؛ لكونها تحت المسلم، وكذلك إذا كانت هي كتابية والزوج مجوسي فأسلم، وقال الشافعي لا يعرض على المصر الإسلام؛ لأن فيه تعريضاً لهم إلا أن ملك النكاح قبل الدخول غير متأكد، فينقطع بنفس الإسلام، وبعده متأكد فيؤجل إلى انقضاء ثلاث حيض، ولنا أن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه فرّق بين نصراني ونصرانية بإبائه عن الإسلام، رواه الطحاوي وأبو بكر بن العربي في "العارضة" أي شرح الترمذي وظهر حكمهم بينهم، ولم ينقل إلينا خلافه فكان إجماعاً، ثم لا فرق بين أن يكون الزوج المصر صبياً مميزاً أو بالغاً حتى يفرق بينهما بإبائه، وهذا على الطرفين، وأما على قول أبي يوسف فقد اختلف المشايخ فيه منهم من يقول: لا يصح إباؤه عنده قياساً على ردته ومنهم من يقول: يصح، - وإباؤه طلاق لا إباؤها، ولو أسلم أحدهما ثمه لم تبن حتى تحيض ثلاثاً، ولو أسلم أي إنكار الزوج عن الإسلام زوج الكتابية بقي نكاحُها. أي أحد الزوجين أي في دار الحرب المرأة، ولو كان أحدهما صغيراً غير مميز ينتظر عقله، بخلاف ما إذا كان مجنوناً حيث لا ينتظر، بل يعرض على أبويه؛ لأنه ليس للجنون نهاية معلومة ثم اعلم أن هذه الفرقة طلاق عند الطرفين إذا أسلمت المرأة فقط، فتعتد إذا أسلمت بعد الدخول بها وأبى الزوج، وعند أبي يوسف: لا تكون طلاقاً بل فسخا؛ لأنه يتصور وجوده من وبمثله لا يقع الطلاق فكان كالفرقة بسبب الملك ولنا أنه لما فات الإمساك بالمعروف وجب التسريح بالإحسان، فإن طلق وإلا ناب القاضي منابه في ذلك، فيكون طلاقاً، بخلاف الملك فإن الفرقة فيه لا بهذا المعنى بل للتنافي، وأما إذ أسلم الزوج فقط فلا تكون الفرقة طلاقاً اتفاقاً. (عيني، فتح) هي لا إباؤها أي لا يكون إباء المرأة طلاقاً بالاتفاق عندنا، وعند الشافعي وقعت الفرقة بإسلام أحدهما إن كان قبل الدخول: وبعده يتوقف كما مر ، ثم إذا وقعت الفرقة بالإباء فإن كان بعد الدخول فلها المهر كله؛ لأنه تأكد بالدخول، وإن كان قبل الدخول فإن كان بإباء الزوج فلها نصف المهر؛ لأنه قبل الدخول، وإن كان بإبائها فلا مهر لها كالردة والمطاوعة رمز الحقائق ٢٢٤/١
If he converts: meaning the other spouse in it, and it is fine and she is his wife. Otherwise, separation is made between them: meaning if the other does not convert and refuses Islam, the judge separates them without delay, whether the refusal was before consummation or after. So for Magians, separation occurs by one's conversion after refusal; for People of the Book, if the wife converts, separation is made; if the husband converts, no action is taken because she remains under a Muslim. Similarly, if she is a Kitabi and he is a Magian and he converts. And al-Shafi'i said: The convert should not be invited to Islam because it exposes him to something, but the marriage bond before consummation is not strong, so it is severed by conversion itself, while after consummation it is strong, so it is delayed until three menstrual periods pass. Our proof is that Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, separated a Christian man and woman due to his refusal to convert, reported by al-Tahawi and Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi in al-'Aridah, and this ruling became known among them and no dissent was transmitted to us, so it is consensus. Then there is no difference between the refusing husband being a discerning minor or an adult; separation is made due to his refusal. This is according to both views. As for Abu Yusuf's opinion, the scholars differed: some say his refusal is invalid by analogy to apostasy, others say it is valid. And his refusal is a talaq, not her refusal. And if one of them converts then she does not become separated until three menstrual periods pass, and if a husband of a Kitabi woman converts, the marriage remains. Meaning one of the spouses in Dar al-Harb, the woman. And if one of them is a minor who does not discern, he is waited for until he reaches understanding, contrary to the insane, where he is not waited for, but it is presented to his parents because insanity has no known end. Then know that this separation is a talaq according to both (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) when the woman converts only; so she observes an 'iddah if she converts after consummation and the husband refuses. According to Abu Yusuf, it is not a talaq but a faskh (annulment), because it can be imagined [to occur in a way] that talaq would not occur, so it is like separation due to ownership. Our proof is that when 'holding with kindness' is impossible, it becomes obligatory to 'release with goodness'; if he does not divorce, the judge acts in his place, so it is a talaq. Unlike ownership, where separation is not in this sense but due to incompatibility. But if the husband only converts, the separation is not talaq by agreement. ('Ayni, Fath) She, not her refusal: meaning the woman's refusal is not talaq by agreement in our school. According to al-Shafi'i, separation occurs by the conversion of one of them if before consummation, and after it is delayed as before. Then when separation occurs by refusal, if after consummation, she gets the full mahr because it is confirmed; if before consummation, if it is by the husband's refusal, she gets half the mahr; if by her refusal, she gets no mahr, like apostasy and mutual compliance. [Symbol of Realities: 224/1]
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling when one polytheist spouse converts to Islam while the other remains. The text clarifies that the judge invites the non-converting spouse to Islam; if they refuse, the judge separates them immediately. The key difference between the schools is that Shafi'i delays the separation after consummation until three menstrual periods, while the Hanafi school relies on the practice of Umar (ra) and considers it consensus, thus immediate. The passage then addresses intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider the separation a talaq (revocable divorce) when the wife converts and the husband refuses, while Abu Yusuf considers it a faskh (annulment). The consequences for mahr are detailed: the wife gets full mahr if separation occurs after consummation, half if before due to husband's refusal, and none if before due to her refusal (like apostasy, which she effectively chose). The text also covers the case of a minor spouse and an insane spouse: a minor who cannot discern is waited until maturity, but an insane person is not waited for; the presentation is made to the parents. The conclusion reiterates that the woman's refusal does not constitute a talaq by agreement in the school.

Key Terms
الإباء (al-ibā'): Refusal to accept Islam when invited after the spouse's conversion.
الفرقة (al-furqa): Judicial separation between spouses due to one's conversion and the other's refusal.
المصر (al-muṣirr): The spouse who persists in disbelief after being invited to Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When one polytheist spouse converts in Dar al-Islam, the judge must invite the other; if they refuse, the judge separates them at once, based on Umar's practice and consensus.

Nature of Separation: Talaq or Faskh? And Mahr Consequences
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the nature of judicial separation and mahr rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the judicial separation is a talaq or faskh, and determine the mahr owed based on timing and who refused.

Core Idea
The separation is a talaq when the wife converts, not when the husband converts, and mahr is only reduced if the wife refuses before consummation.

While the Hanafi school agrees that the judge separates the couple immediately, they differ on the legal nature of that separation: is it a revocable divorce (talaq) or an annulment (faskh)? This lesson focuses on that ikhtilaf and its implications for mahr and 'iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that when the wife converts and the husband refuses, the judicial separation is a talaq. Abu Yusuf considers it a faskh (annulment).
Both agree that when the husband converts and the wife refuses, it is not a talaq by agreement.
2
Analyze the proof for talaq
The majority argue that when 'holding with kindness' is impossible, it is obligatory to 'release with goodness' (Qur'an 2:229). If the husband does not divorce, the judge acts in his place, making the separation a talaq. This analogy fails in ownership-based separation.
The dalil is an implicit qiyas on the judge acting as the husband's deputy.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's rebuttal
Abu Yusuf argues that the separation is like the separation due to ownership (e.g., if one spouse becomes the owner of the other, which is a faskh), not like a divorce.
His reasoning is based on the absence of an explicit divorce pronouncement.
4
Determine the mahr consequences
If separation occurs after consummation, she gets the full mahr. If before consummation and the husband refused, she gets half the mahr. If she refused before consummation, she gets nothing.
The rule for her refusal is analogous to apostasy or mutual compliance.
5
Confirm the agreed point
All Hanafi scholars agree that the woman's refusal does not constitute a talaq; it is simply a cause for separation.
The Shafi'i view on timing is not repeated here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أسلم: أي الأخر فيها ونعمت وهي امرأته (ط، ع) وإلا فرّق بينهما أي وإن لم يسلم الآخر وأبى الإسلام فرق القاضي بينهما ولا يتوقف، سواء كان الإباء قبل الدخول أو بعده، ففي المجوسيين يفرق بإسلام أحدهما بعد الإباء، وفي الكتابيين إن أسلمت الزوجة يفرق بينهما، وإن أسلم الزوج فلا يتعرض لها؛ لكونها تحت المسلم، وكذلك إذا كانت هي كتابية والزوج مجوسي فأسلم، وقال الشافعي لا يعرض على المصر الإسلام؛ لأن فيه تعريضاً لهم إلا أن ملك النكاح قبل الدخول غير متأكد، فينقطع بنفس الإسلام، وبعده متأكد فيؤجل إلى انقضاء ثلاث حيض، ولنا أن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه فرّق بين نصراني ونصرانية بإبائه عن الإسلام، رواه الطحاوي وأبو بكر بن العربي في "العارضة" أي شرح الترمذي وظهر حكمهم بينهم، ولم ينقل إلينا خلافه فكان إجماعاً، ثم لا فرق بين أن يكون الزوج المصر صبياً مميزاً أو بالغاً حتى يفرق بينهما بإبائه، وهذا على الطرفين، وأما على قول أبي يوسف فقد اختلف المشايخ فيه منهم من يقول: لا يصح إباؤه عنده قياساً على ردته ومنهم من يقول: يصح، - وإباؤه طلاق لا إباؤها، ولو أسلم أحدهما ثمه لم تبن حتى تحيض ثلاثاً، ولو أسلم أي إنكار الزوج عن الإسلام زوج الكتابية بقي نكاحُها. أي أحد الزوجين أي في دار الحرب المرأة، ولو كان أحدهما صغيراً غير مميز ينتظر عقله، بخلاف ما إذا كان مجنوناً حيث لا ينتظر، بل يعرض على أبويه؛ لأنه ليس للجنون نهاية معلومة ثم اعلم أن هذه الفرقة طلاق عند الطرفين إذا أسلمت المرأة فقط، فتعتد إذا أسلمت بعد الدخول بها وأبى الزوج، وعند أبي يوسف: لا تكون طلاقاً بل فسخا؛ لأنه يتصور وجوده من وبمثله لا يقع الطلاق فكان كالفرقة بسبب الملك ولنا أنه لما فات الإمساك بالمعروف وجب التسريح بالإحسان، فإن طلق وإلا ناب القاضي منابه في ذلك، فيكون طلاقاً، بخلاف الملك فإن الفرقة فيه لا بهذا المعنى بل للتنافي، وأما إذ أسلم الزوج فقط فلا تكون الفرقة طلاقاً اتفاقاً. (عيني، فتح) هي لا إباؤها أي لا يكون إباء المرأة طلاقاً بالاتفاق عندنا، وعند الشافعي وقعت الفرقة بإسلام أحدهما إن كان قبل الدخول: وبعده يتوقف كما مر ، ثم إذا وقعت الفرقة بالإباء فإن كان بعد الدخول فلها المهر كله؛ لأنه تأكد بالدخول، وإن كان قبل الدخول فإن كان بإباء الزوج فلها نصف المهر؛ لأنه قبل الدخول، وإن كان بإبائها فلا مهر لها كالردة والمطاوعة رمز الحقائق ٢٢٤/١
If he converts: meaning the other spouse in it, and it is fine and she is his wife. Otherwise, separation is made between them: meaning if the other does not convert and refuses Islam, the judge separates them without delay, whether the refusal was before consummation or after. So for Magians, separation occurs by one's conversion after refusal; for People of the Book, if the wife converts, separation is made; if the husband converts, no action is taken because she remains under a Muslim. Similarly, if she is a Kitabi and he is a Magian and he converts. And al-Shafi'i said: The convert should not be invited to Islam because it exposes him to something, but the marriage bond before consummation is not strong, so it is severed by conversion itself, while after consummation it is strong, so it is delayed until three menstrual periods pass. Our proof is that Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, separated a Christian man and woman due to his refusal to convert, reported by al-Tahawi and Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi in al-'Aridah, and this ruling became known among them and no dissent was transmitted to us, so it is consensus. Then there is no difference between the refusing husband being a discerning minor or an adult; separation is made due to his refusal. This is according to both views. As for Abu Yusuf's opinion, the scholars differed: some say his refusal is invalid by analogy to apostasy, others say it is valid. And his refusal is a talaq, not her refusal. And if one of them converts then she does not become separated until three menstrual periods pass, and if a husband of a Kitabi woman converts, the marriage remains. Meaning one of the spouses in Dar al-Harb, the woman. And if one of them is a minor who does not discern, he is waited for until he reaches understanding, contrary to the insane, where he is not waited for, but it is presented to his parents because insanity has no known end. Then know that this separation is a talaq according to both (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) when the woman converts only; so she observes an 'iddah if she converts after consummation and the husband refuses. According to Abu Yusuf, it is not a talaq but a faskh (annulment), because it can be imagined [to occur in a way] that talaq would not occur, so it is like separation due to ownership. Our proof is that when 'holding with kindness' is impossible, it becomes obligatory to 'release with goodness'; if he does not divorce, the judge acts in his place, so it is a talaq. Unlike ownership, where separation is not in this sense but due to incompatibility. But if the husband only converts, the separation is not talaq by agreement. ('Ayni, Fath) She, not her refusal: meaning the woman's refusal is not talaq by agreement in our school. According to al-Shafi'i, separation occurs by the conversion of one of them if before consummation, and after it is delayed as before. Then when separation occurs by refusal, if after consummation, she gets the full mahr because it is confirmed; if before consummation, if it is by the husband's refusal, she gets half the mahr; if by her refusal, she gets no mahr, like apostasy and mutual compliance. [Symbol of Realities: 224/1]
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling when one polytheist spouse converts to Islam while the other remains. The text clarifies that the judge invites the non-converting spouse to Islam; if they refuse, the judge separates them immediately. The key difference between the schools is that Shafi'i delays the separation after consummation until three menstrual periods, while the Hanafi school relies on the practice of Umar (ra) and considers it consensus, thus immediate. The passage then addresses intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider the separation a talaq (revocable divorce) when the wife converts and the husband refuses, while Abu Yusuf considers it a faskh (annulment). The consequences for mahr are detailed: the wife gets full mahr if separation occurs after consummation, half if before due to husband's refusal, and none if before due to her refusal (like apostasy, which she effectively chose). The text also covers the case of a minor spouse and an insane spouse: a minor who cannot discern is waited until maturity, but an insane person is not waited for; the presentation is made to the parents. The conclusion reiterates that the woman's refusal does not constitute a talaq by agreement in the school.

Key Terms
الإباء (al-ibā'): Refusal to accept Islam; the husband's refusal triggers a talaq when the wife converts.
الفرقة (al-furqa): Judicial separation; its nature (talaq or faskh) is the subject of ikhtilaf between the majority and Abu Yusuf.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Annulment of the marriage without the pronouncement of a talaq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what is the legal nature of the judicial separation when the wife converts and the husband refuses?
A) Faskh (annulment).
B) Talaq (divorce).
C) It is not considered a legal separation.
D) It is a khul' (divorce by compensation).

Key Takeaway
The majority Hanafi view considers the judicial separation a talaq when the wife converts, not a faskh, and the mahr is determined by timing and who caused the separation.

Nikah — Marriage

Conversion in Dar al-Harb: Separation After Three Menstruations
Ruling on separation through three menstrual periods in Dar al-Harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for separation after conversion in Dar al-Harb, identify the qiyas basis, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether it constitutes a talaq.

Core Idea
Three menstruations substitute for the judge in Dar al-Harb separation.

This passage addresses a unique scenario: a spouse converts in non-Muslim territory where no Islamic judge exists. Without judicial authority, the standard process of inviting the other spouse and issuing a court order is impossible. The Hanafi school ingeniously substitutes the condition for separation—three menstrual cycles—as the actual separation trigger, drawing an analogy to revocable divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the opening phrase: 'She does not become separated until she menstruates three times.' Note the conditions: both are Kitabi, or one Kitabi and one idol worshipper, or the woman is Muslim. If the husband converts and the wife is Kitabi, the marriage remains.
The ruling does not apply when the wife is Kitabi and husband converts.
2
Contrast the Shafi'i view
The author highlights al-Shafi'i's position: conversion before consummation causes immediate separation; after consummation, separation is delayed until three clean periods. Shafi'i makes no distinction between Dar al-Islam and Dar al-Harb.
The difference stems from Shafi'i's definition of qur' as cleanliness (tuhr) rather than menstruation.
3
Trace the Hanafi rationale and qiyas
The text explains: conversion is obedience, not a cause for separation; the judge lacks jurisdiction in Dar al-Harb; but separation is needed to avoid corruption. Therefore, the condition for separation (three menstruations) is substituted for the separation itself, analogous to revocable divorce where three menstruations are a condition for finality.
This is a clear case of istihsan (juristic preference) based on necessity.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of separation
The passage then asks: Is this separation a talaq? According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (as-Sahibayn), it is a talaq as recorded in as-Siyar al-Kabir; a narration also says they held it to be a separation without talaq, agreeing with Abu Hanifa. The text does not give a definitive tarjih.
The practical consequence: if it is a talaq, it counts toward the three permissible divorces; if not, it is a final separation without affecting the count.
5
Determine the 'iddah consequences
The passage clarifies that these three menstruations do not constitute 'iddah; they are merely a condition for separation. Hence, before consummation, no 'iddah; after consummation with a Harbi woman, no 'iddah; with a Muslim woman, Abu Hanifa says no 'iddah, while as-Sahibayn require 'iddah.
Abu Hanifa's reasoning: since the separation is not a talaq (or at least not a revocable divorce), no 'iddah is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم تبن : المرأة داخل بها أولا. (ط) حتى تحيض ثلاثاً : هذا إذا كانا كتابيين أو كان أحدهما كتابياً والآخر وثنياً، والمرأة المسلمة، أو لم يكونا كتابيين، وأما إذا أسلم الزوج، والزوجة كتابية فهما على نكاحهما على ما مرّ، وقال الشافعي: إن كان إسلام أحدهما قبل الدخول وقعت الفرقة بالإسلام في الحال، وإن كان بعد الدخول يتوقف على مضي ثلاثة قروء (أي أطهار) على ما مر من مذهبه فيما إذا أسلم أحدهما في دار الإسلام، ولا تأثير لاختلاف الدارين عنده، ووجه توقف الفرقة على ثلاث حيض عندنا أن الإسلام ليس سبباً للفرقة؛ لكونه طاعة والعرض على الإسلام متعذر؛ لقصور الولاية في دار الحرب، ولا بد من الفرقة دفعاً للفساد، فأقمنا شرط الفرقة مقام الفرقة، وهو مضي ثلاث حيض؛ لما أن انقضاء ثلاث حيض شرط الفرقة في الطلاق الرجعي. ثم هذه الفرقة هل يكون طلاقاً أم لا؟ فعندهما يكون طلاقاً كما في السير الكبير"، وروي عنهما أنها فرقة بغير طلاق كما هو عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن هذه فرقة وقعت حكماً لا بتفريق القاضي، وكذلك إذا خرج أحدهما إلى دار الإسلام بعد إسلام أحدهما في دار الحرب لا تقع الفرقة بينهما حتى تمضي ثلاث حيض؛ لعدم ولاية القاضي على من بقي في دار الحرب، وهذه الحيض لا تكون عدة ولهذا يستوي فيها المدخول بها وغير المدخول بها، ثم إن كان قبل الدخول فلا عدة عليها وإن كان بعد الدخول والمرأة حربية فكذلك، وإن كانت المسلمة هي فكذلك عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما تحب العدة عليها. (مستخلص، عيني) بقي نكاحها : لأنه يجوز له التزوج بها ابتداء، فالبقاء أولى؛ لأنه أسهل منه. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٢٥/١]
She does not become separated (from her husband) until she menstruates three times: this if both are People of the Book, or if one is a Kitabi and the other an idol worshiper, and the woman is Muslim, or they are not both People of the Book. But if the husband converts and the wife is a Kitabi, they remain in their marriage as previously mentioned. And al-Shafi'i said: If the conversion of one of them was before consummation, the separation occurs immediately with the conversion; if after consummation, it is delayed for three menstrual periods (meaning clean periods), as per his madhhab mentioned earlier when one converts in Dar al-Islam, and in his view, the difference of dar has no effect. The reason for delaying separation until three menstruations in our view is that conversion is not a cause for separation because it is an act of obedience, and presenting Islam is not possible due to the lack of jurisdiction in Dar al-Harb; but separation is necessary to prevent corruption, so we have substituted the condition of separation for the separation itself, which is the passing of three menstruations, because the expiry of three menstruations is a condition for separation in revocable divorce. Then, is this separation a talaq or not? According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is a talaq as in al-Siyar al-Kabir, and it is narrated from them that it is a separation without talaq, as is the view of Abu Hanifa, because this separation occurs by legal ruling, not by the judge's separation. Similarly, if one of them leaves to Dar al-Islam after one converted in Dar al-Harb, separation does not occur until three menstruations pass, due to the lack of the judge's authority over the one remaining in Dar al-Harb. And these menstruations do not constitute an 'iddah, therefore the one who has been consummated and the one who has not consummated are equal in this. Then if it was before consummation, there is no 'iddah; if after consummation and the woman is a Harbi, likewise; if the Muslim woman is the one, likewise according to Abu Hanifa, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), 'iddah becomes obligatory upon her. (Mukhtalaf, 'Ayni) The marriage remains: because it is permissible for him to marry her initially, so remaining is more appropriate as it is easier. [Symbol of Realities: 225/1]
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage addresses the unique situation of conversion in Dar al-Harb (non-Muslim territory). Since the judge has no jurisdiction there, the standard procedure of invitation and judicial separation is impossible. Therefore, the Hanafi school substitutes the passage of three menstrual periods as a condition for the separation to occur, essentially acting as a proxy for the judge's ruling. The text first clarifies the case: this two-stage process applies when the wife is Muslim after conversion or when the spouses are Kitabi or one is Kitabi and other Majusi, but not when the husband converts and the wife is Kitabi (marriage remains). Then the Shafi'i view is contrasted: Shafi'i does not distinguish between Dar al-Islam and Dar al-Harb regarding the timing of separation (immediate before consummation, delayed by three clean periods after). The Hanafi rationale is then explained: conversion is obedience, not a cause for separation; but separation is needed to avoid corruption; since the judge cannot act, the condition for separation (three menstruations) is made the agent of separation. The text then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold this separation is a talaq (as in al-Siyar al-Kabir), while Abu Hanifa considers it a separation not by talaq because it occurs by legal effect rather than judicial pronouncement. The passage also discusses the consequences for 'iddah: according to Abu Hanifa, no 'iddah is required even after consummation, while the two imams require 'iddah for the Muslim woman. The text ends by affirming that the marriage of a Kitabi wife remains valid when her husband converts, because initial marriage was permissible, and continuity is easier than establishing anew.

Key Terms
القروء (al-qurū'): Menstrual periods or clean periods; here in the context of Shafi'i's view, it refers to three clean periods (tuhr).
العدة (al-'iddah): The waiting period after divorce or separation. In this passage, the text specifies that these three menstruations do not constitute 'iddah; they are merely a condition for separation.
فرقة (firqah): Separation between spouses. In this passage, it is used to denote the legal termination of the marriage.
طلاق (talāq): Divorce pronounced by the husband or his delegate. The dispute is whether this separation counts as a talaq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens when a Muslim woman converts to Islam in Dar al-Harb while her husband remains a non-Muslim, and there is no judge available?
A) The marriage is annulled immediately because she became Muslim.
B) The marriage continues until they leave to Dar al-Islam.
C) The wife is separated from her husband after she menstruates three times, and no 'iddah is required according to Abu Hanifa.
D) The husband is invited to Islam and if he refuses, the judge separates them.

Key Takeaway
In Dar al-Harb, three menstrual periods substitute for the judge's decree and cause separation, but not necessarily as a talaq; 'iddah is waived according to Abu Hanifa.

Nikah — Marriage

Ruling on Husband's Refusal upon Wife's Islam and the Nature of the Separation (Talaq or Faskh?)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of separation from conversion in Dar al-Harb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the husband's refusal being talaq, explain the waiting period in Dar al-Harb, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the separation is talaq or faskh.

Core Idea
The husband's refusal to convert after the wife's Islam is talaq; the wife's refusal is not.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a critical issue in interfaith marriages: when one spouse converts to Islam in Dar al-Harb, how does the marriage dissolve? The Hanafi school distinguishes between the husband's and wife's refusal, and within the school there is a dispute on the legal nature of the separation. Understanding this ruling requires careful attention to the conditions of iddah and the role of the judge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule on refusal
Determine the ruling when one spouse converts and the other refuses. The passage states: 'إباؤه طلاق لا إباؤها' — the husband's refusal is talaq, not the wife's.
This is a consensus among Hanafis.
2
Understand the waiting period in Dar al-Harb
When conversion occurs in Dar al-Harb, separation does not happen immediately but is suspended until three menstruations pass. This is because the judge lacks jurisdiction and Islam is not a cause of separation.
Contrast with Shafi'i view of immediate separation before consummation.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of the separation
Distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa (faskh) and Abu Yusuf & Muhammad (talaq). Note the reasoning: Abu Hanifa analogizes to separation due to ownership (milk); the Sahibayn use istihsan arguing the judge substitutes for the husband's divorce.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is the Sahibayn's: it is talaq.
4
Apply the practical consequence: iddah
If the separation is talaq, iddah is required after consummation. If faskh, according to Abu Hanifa, no iddah is required. The mufta bihi requires iddah.
This highlights the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
5
Contrast with the case of husband's conversion only
When only the husband converts, the separation is not talaq by consensus. The wife's refusal is also not talaq.
This is consistent with the general rule that the wife's refusal has no effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب نكاح الكافر
وإباؤه طلاق لا إباؤها، ولو أسلم أحدهما ثمه لم تبن حتى تحيض ثلاثاً، ولو أسلم زوج
أي إنكار الزوج عن الإسلام
زوج
الكتابية بقي نكاحُها.
أي أحد الزوجين أي في دار الحرب
المرأة،
ولو كان أحدهما صغيراً غير مميز ينتظر عقله، بخلاف ما إذا كان مجنوناً حيث لا ينتظر، بل يعرض على أبويه؛ لأنه ليس للجنون نهاية معلومة ثم اعلم أن هذه الفرقة طلاق عند الطرفين إذا أسلمت المرأة فقط، فتعتد إذا أسلمت بعد الدخول بها وأبى الزوج، وعند أبي يوسف: لا تكون طلاقاً بل فسخا؛ لأنه يتصور وجوده من وبمثله لا يقع الطلاق فكان كالفرقة بسبب الملك ولنا أنه لما فات الإمساك بالمعروف وجب التسريح بالإحسان، فإن طلق وإلا ناب القاضي منابه في ذلك، فيكون طلاقاً، بخلاف الملك فإن الفرقة فيه لا بهذا المعنى بل للتنافي، وأما إذ أسلم الزوج فقط فلا تكون الفرقة طلاقاً اتفاقاً. (عيني، فتح)
هي
لا إباؤها أي لا يكون إباء المرأة طلاقاً بالاتفاق عندنا، وعند الشافعي وقعت الفرقة بإسلام أحدهما إن كان قبل الدخول: وبعده يتوقف كما مر ، ثم إذا وقعت الفرقة بالإباء فإن كان بعد الدخول فلها المهر كله؛ لأنه تأكد بالدخول، وإن كان قبل الدخول فإن كان بإباء الزوج فلها نصف المهر؛ لأنه قبل الدخول، وإن كان بإبائها فلا مهر لها كالردة والمطاوعة رمز الحقائق ٢٢٤/١٥] لم تبن : المرأة داخل بها أولا. (ط) حتى تحيض ثلاثاً : هذا إذا كانا كتابيين أو كان أحدهما كتابياً والآخر وثنياً، والمرأة . المسلمة، أو لم يكونا كتابيين، وأما إذا أسلم الزوج، والزوجة كتابية فهما على نكاحهما على ما مرّ، وقال الشافعي: إن كان إسلام أحدهما قبل الدخول وقعت الفرقة بالإسلام في الحال، وإن كان بعد الدخول يتوقف على مضي ثلاثة قروء (أي أطهار) على ما مر من مذهبه فيما إذا أسلم أحدهما في دار الإسلام، ولا تأثير لاختلاف الدارين عنده، ووجه توقف الفرقة على ثلاث حيض عندنا أن الإسلام ليس سبباً للفرقة؛ لكونه طاعة والعرض على الإسلام متعذر؛ لقصور الولاية في دار الحرب، ولا بد من الفرقة دفعاً للفساد، فأقمنا شرط الفرقة مقام الفرقة، وهو مضي ثلاث حيض؛ لما أن انقضاء ثلاث حيض شرط الفرقة في الطلاق الرجعي. ثم هذه الفرقة هل يكون طلاقاً أم لا؟ فعندهما يكون طلاقاً كما في السير الكبير"، وروي عنهما أنها فرقة بغير طلاق كما هو عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن هذه فرقة وقعت حكماً لا بتفريق القاضي، وكذلك إذا خرج أحدهما إلى دار الإسلام بعد إسلام أحدهما في دار الحرب لا تقع الفرقة بينهما حتى تمضي ثلاث حيض؛ لعدم ولاية القاضي على من بقي في دار الحرب، وهذه الحيض لا تكون عدة ولهذا يستوي فيها المدخول بها وغير المدخول بها، ثم إن كان قبل الدخول فلا عدة عليها وإن كان بعد الدخول والمرأة حربية فكذلك، وإن كانت المسلمة هي فكذلك عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما تحب العدة عليها. (مستخلص، عيني)
Chapter of Marriage of the Disbeliever. And his refusal (the husband's) is divorce, not her refusal. And if one of them converts [to Islam] then [the marriage] is not dissolved until she has completed three menstruations. And if the husband of a Kitabiyya converts, his marriage remains. (Meaning: one of the two spouses, i.e., in Dar al-Harb; the woman). And if one of them is a minor without discernment, he is waited for until he gains reason, unlike if he is insane, where he is not waited for, but rather it is presented to his parents because insanity has no known end. Then know that this separation is divorce according to both [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] when only the woman converts, so she observes iddah if she converted after consummation and the husband refused. According to Abu Yusuf: it is not divorce but annulment (faskh) because it can be conceived of occurring from something by which divorce does not occur, so it is like separation due to ownership. And our proof: since retention with kindness is lost, release with kindness becomes obligatory; if he divorces, [it is done]; otherwise the judge substitutes for him in that, so it becomes divorce. Unlike ownership, where separation is not in this sense but due to incompatibility. And when only the husband converts, the separation is not divorce by consensus. (Ayni, Fath) 'Her refusal' means the woman's refusal is not divorce by consensus with us. According to al-Shafi'i: the separation occurs by the conversion of one of them if before consummation; after consummation it is suspended as previously. Then when separation occurs due to refusal, if after consummation she gets the full mahr because it is confirmed by consummation; if before consummation, if by the husband's refusal she gets half the mahr; if by her refusal, no mahr for her like apostasy and willingness. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 224/15) 'She is not separated': whether the woman has been consummated or not. (T) 'Until she menstruates three times': this is if both are Kitabi or one is Kitabi and the other idolater, and the woman is Muslim, or if neither is Kitabi. But if the husband converts and the wife is Kitabi, they remain in their marriage as previously. Al-Shafi'i said: if the conversion of one is before consummation, the separation occurs by conversion immediately; if after consummation, it depends on the passing of three menstruations (or purity periods) according to his school when one converts in Dar al-Islam, and difference of abodes has no effect with him. The reason for suspending the separation on three menstruations with us is that Islam is not a cause for separation because it is an act of obedience, and presenting Islam is impossible due to guardianship deficiency in Dar al-Harb, and separation is necessary to avoid corruption. So we made the condition of separation stand in place of the separation, which is the passing of three menstruations, because the expiration of three menstruations is a condition for separation in revocable divorce. Then this separation: is it divorce or not? According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is divorce as in al-Siyar al-Kabir, and it is narrated from them that it is a separation without divorce, as is the view of Abu Hanifa, because this separation occurs by legal ruling not by the judge's decree. Similarly, if one of them goes to Dar al-Islam after the conversion of one in Dar al-Harb, separation does not occur until three menstruations pass, due to lack of authority of the judge over the one remaining in Dar al-Harb. And these menstruations are not iddah; hence the consummated and unconsummated are equal in this. Then if it was before consummation, there is no iddah for her; if after consummation and the woman is a Harbiyya, similarly; and if she is Muslim, similarly according to Abu Hanifa, but according to them iddah is obligatory upon her. (Mustakhlas, Ayni)
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute combined with an inter-madhhab comparison regarding the separation that occurs when one spouse converts to Islam in Dar al-Harb. The passage opens with the ruling that the husband's refusal (to convert) after the wife's conversion constitutes a divorce (talaq), but the wife's refusal does not. This is a consensus among the Hanafis. The passage then discusses the waiting period: if one converts in Dar al-Harb, separation does not happen immediately but after three menstruations. This differs from the Shafi'i view where separation is immediate before consummation and suspended after. The Hanafi reason is that Islam is not a cause of separation, and since the judge has no jurisdiction in Dar al-Harb, the condition of separation (three menstruations) substitutes for the actual separation. The passage then delves into the nature of the separation: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), the separation is a divorce (talaq) based on the principle of istihsan, because the judge steps in to issue the divorce if the husband refuses. According to Abu Hanifa, it is not a divorce but an annulment (faskh) because it occurs by legal ruling without the judge's actual decree. Abu Yusuf initially held it to be faskh but his later view (the one recorded in the passage) is that it is talaq. The practical implication is seen in iddah: according to Abu Hanifa, no iddah is required for the woman even after consummation, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require iddah. The passage also clarifies that if only the husband converts, the separation is not divorce by consensus. Throughout, the commentator (Ayni) provides the evidence and reasoning.

Key Terms
إباء (Iba'): Refusal of the non-Muslim spouse to accept Islam when the other spouse converts.
فرقة (Furqa): Legal separation between spouses due to conversion or other causes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the nature of the separation when the wife converts and the husband refuses in Dar al-Harb?
A) It is faskh (annulment), so no iddah is required.
B) It is talaq (divorce), so iddah is required after consummation.
C) The marriage continues until the husband also converts.
D) The separation is immediate and the wife must observe iddah.

Key Takeaway
The husband's refusal after the wife's Islam is a talaq according to the relied-upon Hanafi view, and the wife must observe iddah if the marriage was consummated; the waiting period of three menstruations substitutes for the lack of judicial authority in Dar al-Harb.

Validity of a Kitabiyya Marriage After Husband's Conversion
Ruling statement with brief proof from Kanz al-Daqaiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a Kitabiyya marriage when the husband converts to Islam and explain the legal reasoning based on the principle of continuity (al-baqa' awla).

Core Idea
A marriage with a Kitabiyya persists after the husband's conversion because what is permissible to initiate is easier to preserve.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in interfaith marriages: when a non-Muslim husband embraces Islam, what happens to his existing marriage if his wife is a Kitabiyya (Jewish or Christian)? The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling grounded in a core legal maxim. The passage you'll study is from Kanz al-Daqaiq, a foundational matn on Hanafi fiqh, with its commentary Ramz al-Haqaiq. It succinctly states the ruling and its rationale, offering a window into how Hanafi scholars reason from permissibility of initiation to continuity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
What exactly does the passage rule on? Identify the legal scenario and the outcome for the marriage.
The scenario: a non-Muslim husband converts to Islam while his wife remains a Kitabiyya.
2
Analyze the Reasoning
The passage gives a brief proof: 'because it is permissible for him to marry her initially, so continuing is more appropriate.' Break down this argument. What principle is being applied?
The key maxim is 'al-baqa' awla' – continuity is more appropriate/easier than initiation.
3
Locate the Hukm Level
Determine the legal status of the marriage after conversion. Is it sahih (valid) or something else? Why?
The marriage is sahih; there is no need for a new contract.
4
Apply to a New Scenario
Consider: what if the wife converts before the husband? Would the ruling be the same? Use the same principle.
If the wife converts first and the husband remains non-Muslim, the marriage is not automatically valid because a Muslim woman cannot marry a non-Muslim. The principle works only in one direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي نكاحها : لأنه يجوز له التزوج بها ابتداء، فالبقاء أولى؛ لأنه أسهل منه. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٢٥/١]
Her marriage remains: because it is permissible for him to marry her initially, so continuing is more appropriate; because it is easier than that. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 225/1)
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that clarifies why a marriage with a Kitabiyya woman remains valid if the husband converts to Islam. The reasoning is based on a principle of prioritizing ease: since a Muslim man can initially marry a Kitabiyya woman (a Jewish or Christian woman), it follows that if he was a non-Muslim and then converts, the existing marriage should be preserved rather than requiring a new contract. The phrase 'البقاء أولى' (continuation is more appropriate) is a key legal maxim in Hanafi fiqh regarding continuity of rights.

Key Terms
كتابية (Kitabiyya): A woman from the People of the Book (Jew or Christian) whom a Muslim man is permitted to marry.
البقاء أولى (al-baqa' awla): A Hanafi legal maxim meaning 'continuity is more appropriate/easier' – used to justify preserving an existing state rather than requiring a new action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a marriage with a Kitabiyya remain valid after the husband's conversion to Islam?
A) Because the wife must also convert for the marriage to continue.
B) Because a Muslim man can marry a Kitabiyya initially, so continuity is easier.
C) Because the marriage was contracted under Islamic law from the start.
D) Because the husband's conversion nullifies all previous contracts.

Key Takeaway
A Kitabiyya marriage remains valid after the husband's conversion because Hanafi fiqh prioritizes continuity over renewal when the initial act is permissible.

Iddah of the Muhajira: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Ruling on emigrant woman's marriage without iddah and the dispute with the Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on iddah for a non-pregnant muhajira and explain the legal proof (dalil) for each position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: no iddah; Sahibayn: iddah required; the dispute turns on whether the Quranic permission is absolute.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute over whether an emigrant woman (muhajira) who leaves Dar al-Harb to settle in Dar al-Islam must observe iddah before remarrying. Abu Hanifa holds that a non-pregnant muhajira may marry immediately, while his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), insist on iddah. The debate hinges on the interpretation of a Quranic verse and the principle of adding to the text (ziyada 'ala al-nass).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
The act is remarrying a muhajira (emigrant woman) who is non-pregnant. The dispute is whether iddah is required before that marriage.
The term 'muhajira' is specifically defined as a woman who leaves Dar al-Harb with intention to settle permanently in Dar al-Islam.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa says no iddah is required. He cites Quran 60:10: 'There is no sin upon you that you marry them' as an unconditional permission. He argues that requiring iddah would be an addition to the text (ziyada) which amounts to abrogation (naskh) without evidence.
The verse is mutlaq (absolute) and Hanafi usul holds that a mutlaq text cannot be restricted without a separate proof.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say iddah is obligatory. Their reasoning: the divorce or separation occurred after the marriage had been consummated in Dar al-Islam, so the woman is now subject to Islamic law which requires iddah for all divorced women.
The Sahibayn rely on the general principle that any woman divorced after consummation must observe iddah.
4
Analyse the exception for pregnant muhajira
The passage notes that even Abu Hanifa agrees a pregnant muhajira may not marry until she gives birth. This is a precaution (ihtiyat) to protect lineage, not an iddah.
This is the relied-upon view (al-ashah wa zahir al-riwaya): she cannot remarry until after delivery, but it is not considered iddah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the woman can marry immediately after arrival; under the Sahibayn, she must wait for three menstrual cycles (or months) before remarrying.
The mufta bihi (operative opinion) in later Hanafi fatwa may lean towards Abu Hanifa's view due to the clear Quranic text, but caution is recommended in many contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المهاجرة: أي التاركة دار الحرب إلى دار إسلام على قصد عدم العود سواء كانت مسلمة أو ذمية (ط) الحائل ضد الحامل يعني غير الحبلى مسلمة كانت أو ذمية، قيد بالحائل؛ لأن الحبلى لا يجوز تزوجها حتى تضع. (ع)
وهم
بلا عدة: أي تنكح المهاجرة غير الحبلى بلا عدة عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: تلزمها العدة؛ لأن الفرقة وقعت بعد الدخول في دار الإسلام، فيلزمها حكم الإسلام، ولأبي حنيفة قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْكُمْ أَنْ تَنْكِحُوهُنَّ (الممتحنة: ١٠) حيث أباح نكاح المهاجرات مطلقاً، فتقييده بما بعد العدة زيادة على النص، وهي نسخ، أما إذا كانت حاملاً فلا نقول بوجوب العدة عليها، ولكنها لا تنكح ما لم تضع حملها؛ لأن في بطنها ولداً ثابت النسب، فظهر في حق المنع احتياطاً وهو الأصح وظاهر الرواية، وقيد بالمهاجرة؛ لأن التي طلقت في دار الحرب لا عدة عليها اتفاقاً، وبوجوب العدة على المهاجرة قالت الثلاثة. (عيني، فتح)
Al-Muhajira: meaning she who leaves Dar al-Harb to Dar al-Islam with the intention of not returning, whether she is Muslim or dhimmi. (T) Al-Ha'il: opposite of pregnant, i.e., non-pregnant, whether Muslim or dhimmi. It is restricted to non-pregnant because the pregnant woman may not be married until she gives birth. (A) And they (the Sahibayn) said: 'Without iddah' meaning the muhajira who is not pregnant may be married without iddah according to Abu Hanifa. But according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), iddah is incumbent upon her because the separation occurred after consummation in Dar al-Islam, so the ruling of Islam applies to her. For Abu Hanifa, the proof is the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'There is no sin upon you that you marry them' (al-Mumtahinah 60:10), where He permitted marrying the emigrant women absolutely. Restricting it to after iddah would be an addition to the text, which is abrogation (naskh). However, if she is pregnant, we do not say iddah is obligatory, but she may not marry until she delivers, because there is a child in her belly with established lineage, so prohibition appears as a precaution, and this is the most correct and the apparent of the narration. It is restricted to muhajira because the one divorced in Dar al-Harb has no iddah by consensus. The three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i) said that iddah is obligatory on the muhajira. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether an emigrant woman (muhajira) who leaves Dar al-Harb for Dar al-Islam must observe iddah before remarrying. Abu Hanifa rules that no iddah is required for a non-pregnant woman, citing the Quranic verse 'There is no sin upon you that you marry them' which is unconditional. He argues that imposing iddah would be an addition to the text (ziyada 'ala al-nass) which amounts to abrogation. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require iddah because the separation occurred after consummation and she is now subject to Islamic law. The passage clarifies that this applies only to non-pregnant women; a pregnant muhajira may not marry until she delivers, as a precaution to avoid confusion of lineage. This is the relied-upon view (al-ashah wa zahir al-riwaya). The term muhajira is specified because a woman divorced in Dar al-Harb has no iddah by consensus. The three (Sahibayn and Shafi'i) all require iddah for the muhajira. The Hanafi position is supported by the Quranic text.

Key Terms
المهاجرة (al-Muhajira): A woman who leaves Dar al-Harb for Dar al-Islam with the intention of permanent settlement, whether she is Muslim or dhimmi.
الحائل (al-Ha'il): A non-pregnant woman; the opposite of pregnant (hubla).
الصاحبان (al-Sahibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two leading disciples of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence Abu Hanifa uses to support his view that a non-pregnant muhajira can marry without iddah?
A) The consensus of the Companions
B) The Quranic verse 'There is no sin upon you that you marry them'
C) Analogy to a woman divorced in Dar al-Harb
D) The principle of istihsan

Key Takeaway
A non-pregnant muhajira may remarry immediately according to Abu Hanifa due to the unconditional Quranic permission, but the Sahibayn require iddah because the separation occurred in Dar al-Islam.

Apostasy of One Spouse: Faskh or Talaq?
Ruling on apostasy with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on apostasy of a spouse and distinguish between the views of the two shaykhs and Imam Muhammad on whether it is faskh or talaq.

Core Idea
Apostasy of either spouse annuls the marriage immediately (faskh), not by talaq.

When one spouse leaves Islam, what happens to the marriage? The Hanafi school has a clear position, but there is an internal dispute on the legal nature of the separation. This passage explains the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that it is an immediate annulment (faskh), while Imam Muhammad analogizes it to a case of refusal. Understanding this distinction is crucial for knowing whether the separation counts as a divorce or not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the passage to determine what happens to the marriage when one spouse apostatizes. The text says 'فسخ في الحال' (annulment immediately).
The key term is faskh, not talaq.
2
Note the scope
The ruling applies whether the apostasy occurs before or after consummation. The text says 'سواء كان قبل الدخول أو بعده'.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold the absolute view of faskh. Imam Muhammad holds that if the husband apostatizes, it is talaq by analogy with the case of iba' (refusal to convert).
The text says 'وهذا بإطلاقه عند الشيخين' and then mentions Muhammad's view.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Abu Hanifa distinguishes apostasy from iba' because apostasy is inherently contradictory to marriage (since it is contradictory to Islam). Muhammad analogizes to iba' where the husband's refusal is treated as talaq.
The text mentions 'اعتباراً بالإباء'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the view of the two shaykhs, i.e., immediate faskh. This is the majority and authoritative view.
The text gives authority to the two shaykhs by saying 'وهذا بإطلاقه عند الشيخين'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فسخ في الحال: [سواء كان قبل الدخول أو بعده (ط)] أي إذا ارتد أحد الزوجين عن الإسلام فسخ النكاح في الحال أي فرق بينهما بغير طلاق، وهذا بإطلاقه عند الشيخين، وقال محمد: إن كانت الردة من الزوج فهي فرقة بطلاق اعتباراً بالإباء، وأبو يوسف مر على أصله الذي كان له في الإباء، وأبو حنيفة فرق بأن الردة منافية للنكاح لكونها منافية =
Annulment (faskh) immediately: [whether before or after consummation (T)] i.e., if one of the spouses apostatizes from Islam, the marriage is annulled immediately, meaning they are separated without divorce. This is absolute according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Muhammad said: if the apostasy is from the husband, it is a separation by divorce, by analogy with refusal (iba'). Abu Yusuf followed his principle that he had in refusal. Abu Hanifa distinguished by saying that apostasy is contradictory to marriage because it is contradictory to ... (incomplete)
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of apostasy (ridda) on marriage. According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), apostasy of either spouse causes immediate annulment (faskh) of the marriage, not a divorce (talaq). This means the separation occurs automatically and does not count towards the three divorces. However, Imam Muhammad differs: he analogizes apostasy to the case of 'iba' (refusal to convert), where the husband's refusal is considered a talaq. Therefore, in his view, if the husband apostatizes, it is a talaq; but if the wife apostatizes, it remains faskh? The text is cut off, but it implies that Abu Yusuf applied his same principle from the iba' case (where he considered it talaq) to apostasy, but here the text says he agreed with Abu Hanifa on faskh? Actually it says 'وهذا بإطلاقه عند الشيخين' meaning the absolute ruling of faskh is from the two shaykhs. Then it says 'وأبو يوسف مر على أصله الذي كان له في الإباء' but that contradicts? Perhaps it means he abandoned his earlier view? The commentary is incomplete, but the key point is that the relied-upon view is faskh immediately.

Key Terms
الردة (al-Ridda): Apostasy from Islam; leaving the faith. It is a cause for immediate dissolution of marriage according to most Hanafis.
فسخ (Faskh): Legal annulment of a contract; in this context, dissolution of the marriage contract without it being a divorce (talaq).
الإباء (al-Iba'): Refusal of a non-Muslim spouse to embrace Islam after the other spouse has converted. In Hanafi law, the husband's refusal may result in talaq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what is the effect on the marriage if one spouse apostatizes from Islam?
A) The marriage is immediately annulled (faskh) without divorce.
B) The husband's apostasy counts as one divorce (talaq).
C) The marriage is suspended until the apostate repents.
D) The marriage continues if the apostate returns to Islam within three days.

Key Takeaway
Apostasy of either spouse causes immediate annulment (faskh) of the marriage, not talaq, according to the relied-upon Hanafi view.

10 min
Cause of Separation: Difference of Abodes vs. Captivity
Inter-madhhab dispute on cause of separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the cause of marital separation across abodes and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
تباين الدارين alone dissolves marriage; captivity does not, because it contradicts initiation but not continuation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a key inter-madhhab dispute: does marriage automatically dissolve when one spouse moves from Dar al-Harb to Dar al-Islam? The Hanafi school answers yes, based on the impossibility of maintaining marital interests, while al-Shafi'i holds that only actual captivity ends the marriage. Understanding this dispute reveals how each school weighs effective causes (ʿilla) and textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling in the matn: 'tabyīn al-dārayn is the cause of separation, not captivity.' Note that the Hanafis consider the difference of abodes itself sufficient to dissolve the marriage immediately.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The passage mentions al-Shafi'i (followed by Malik and Ahmad) who hold that captivity (sabi) is the cause, not tabyīn al-dārayn. Understand the disagreement: for the opponent, marriage continues despite difference of abodes unless one spouse is captured.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate and based on different interpretations of evidence.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent argues that tabyīn al-dārayn only ends guardianship (wilāya), not marriage. They cite the Prophetic precedent of returning Zainab to her husband with the first contract, and argue that captivity gives ownership (milk) to the captor, dissolving marriage.
Zainab's case is central to the dispute.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response has three parts: 1) The interests of marriage (masāliḥ al-nikāḥ) cannot be maintained with difference of abodes, so it is the effective cause. 2) Captivity does not contradict initiating marriage, so it does not contradict its continuation (qiyas). 3) The report about Zainab's return with the first contract is weak; a stronger report indicates a new contract.
Note the use of qiyas and hadith criticism.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: If a Muslim from Dar al-Harb emigrates to Dar al-Islam while his non-Muslim wife remains, according to Hanafis the marriage ends immediately; according to Shafi'is it continues unless the wife is also captured. If both are captured together, Hanafis say marriage continues, Shafi'is say it ends.
This affects remarriage, inheritance, and custody.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتباين الدارين سبب الفرقة لا السبي. وتباين الدارين سبب الفرقة : [ أي سبب وقوع الفرقة عندنا، حتى إذا خرج أحد الزوجين إلى دار الإسلام مسلما أو ذميًا وقعت البينونة. (ع)] عندنا، وقال الشافعي: سبب الفرقة هو السبي دون تباين الدارين، حتى إذا أحد الزوجين إلينا من دار الحرب مسلماً وقعت الفرقة بينهما عندنا وعند الشافعي: لا تقع، ولو سبي خرج أحد الزوجين الحربيين وجيء إلى دار الإسلام تقع الفرقة بينهما اتفاقاً؛ لتباين الدارين عندنا وللسبي عند الشافعي، وإن سبيا معا لم تقع الفرقة بينهما عندنا وعند الشافعي: تقع، وبقوله قال مالك ،وأحمد، قالوا: إن تباين الدارين يظهر في انقطاع الولاية، ولا أثر لذلك في وقوع الفرقة، وأما السبي فيقتضي خلوص الملك للسابي وهو مستلزم لانقطاع ملك النكاح، قلنا: إن مصالح النكاح لا تنتظم حقيقة ولا حكما مع تباين الدارين، والسبي يوجب ملك الرقبة، وهو لا ينافي ابتداء النكاح فكذا لا ينافي بقاءه. فإن قلت: رده عليه بنته زينب إلى زوجها بالعقد الأول دليل على ما ذكروا؟ قلت: روي أنه علي ردها بعقد جديد فكان المثبت أولى من النافي على أن ما رووه غير صحيح عند أهل النقل فلا يعارض ما روينا لصحته، فإن قلت في ما رويتم حجاج وهو متكلم فيه؟ قلت: هذا جرح مبهم فلا يصح وقد وثقه أهل النقل حتى خرج له مسلم، ولأن ما رووه متروك الظاهر؛ لأنه ذكر فيه أن إسلامها كان قبل إسلامه بست سنين، وقيل: بسنتين لا يرون بقاء النكاح بعد انقضاء عدتها قبل إسلام المتأخر منهما، وإن قيل: قد أباح عليه وطء سبايا أوطاس بعد الاستبراء وقد سبين مع أزواجهن قلت لا نسلم بل سبين وحدهن؛ لأن رجالهن قتلوا. (عيني) لا السبي: أي ليس السبي سبب الفرقة عندنا . (ع)
Difference of the two abodes is a cause of separation, not captivity. [Commentary] Difference of the two abodes is a cause: Meaning the cause of separation according to us, so that when one of the two spouses leaves to the Abode of Islam as a Muslim or a dhimmi, separation occurs. According to us, and al-Shafi'i said: The cause of separation is captivity, not difference of abodes. So that when one of the two spouses comes to us from the Abode of War as a Muslim, separation occurs between them according to us, but according to al-Shafi'i it does not occur. And if one of the two warring spouses is captured and brought to the Abode of Islam, separation occurs by agreement – due to difference of abodes according to us and due to captivity according to al-Shafi'i. And if both are captured together, separation does not occur according to us, but according to al-Shafi'i it does occur. Malik and Ahmad said according to his (al-Shafi'i's) statement. They said: Difference of abodes appears in the termination of guardianship, and that has no effect in the occurrence of separation. As for captivity, it necessitates the purity of ownership for the captor, and that entails the termination of the ownership of marriage. We said: The interests of marriage cannot be arranged in reality or by legal ruling with difference of abodes. Captivity necessitates ownership of the person (riqab), and that does not contradict initiating marriage, so likewise it does not contradict its continuation. If you say: His (the Prophet's) returning his daughter Zainab to her husband with the first contract is evidence for what they mentioned? I say: It is narrated that he returned her with a new contract, so the affirmatory is preferable to the negatory. Moreover, what they narrated is not authentic according to the people of transmission, so it does not contradict what we narrated due to its authenticity. If you say: Regarding what you narrated, there is Hajjaj, and he is criticized? I say: This is an ambiguous disparagement, so it is not valid. The people of transmission have declared him trustworthy until Muslim narrated from him. Also, what they narrated is abandoned in its apparent meaning because it mentions that her Islam was six years before his Islam, or it is said two years after; they do not consider the marriage remaining after the expiry of her waiting period before the Islam of the later one. And if it is said: He (the Prophet) permitted intercourse with the captives of Autoas after istibra, and they were captured with their husbands? I say: We do not accept it; rather they were captured alone because their men were killed. (Ayni) 'Not captivity': i.e., captivity is not a cause of separation according to us.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the cause of separation when one spouse leaves Dar al-Harb for Dar al-Islam. The Hanafi position, stated in the matn, is that tabyīn al-dārayn (difference of abodes) itself causes separation, not captivity. The commentator explains that according to the Hanafis, if one spouse emigrates to Dar al-Islam (whether Muslim or dhimmi), separation occurs immediately. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, holding that only captivity (sabi) causes separation. The Hanafis argue that the interests of marriage (masāliḥ al-nikāḥ) cannot be maintained when the spouses are in different abodes, as there is no effective guardianship or mutual rights. Captivity, on the other hand, gives ownership of the person to the captor, but that does not contradict the continuation of marriage; it only contradicts initiating marriage in the first place, but since marriage already exists, captivity does not nullify it. The passage also rebuts the Shafi'i evidence from the Prophet's returning his daughter Zainab to her husband (Abu al-Aas) without a new contract, arguing that the report is weak and that a stronger report indicates a new contract. The Hanafi position is that tabyīn al-dārayn is the effective cause (ʿilla) of separation, and this is established by istihsan based on the impossibility of maintaining marital interests across warring territories.

Key Terms
تباين الدارين (tabyīn al-dārayn): Difference of the two abodes: the situation where one spouse is in Dar al-Islam and the other in Dar al-Harb, with no effective communication or legal protection.
السبي (al-sabi): Captivity: being taken as a prisoner of war and thus becoming owned as a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Captivity (sabi)
B) Difference of abodes (tabyīn al-dārayn)
C) Both together
D) The spouse's conversion to Islam

Key Takeaway
تباين الدارين alone dissolves marriage according to Hanafis; captivity does not, because it does not contradict the continuation of marriage.

Effect of Apostasy on Marriage and Mahr
Ruling on apostasy causing immediate separation and mahr consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that apostasy dissolves marriage immediately (faskh) and apply the mahr rules to each of the four scenarios based on who apostatizes and when.

Core Idea
Apostasy dissolves marriage instantly, with mahr dependent on who apostatizes and when.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on apostasy (riddah) and its immediate effect on the marriage contract. Unlike divorce, apostasy renders the marriage invalid instantly, and the mahr obligations vary based on specific scenarios. We will explore the reasoning behind the Hanafi view, the intra-madhhab difference, and the practical application of the mahr rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on apostasy
State the Hanafi position that apostasy causes immediate dissolution (faskh) without divorce (talaq), whether before or after consummation.
The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) agree on this; Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof
Abu Hanifa differentiates apostasy from i‘bā’ (refusal to convert): apostasy contradicts the sanctity (ʿisma) of marriage, while i‘bā’ only prevents harmonious retention, so apostasy cannot be treated as talaq.
I‘bā’ requires judicial separation; apostasy does not.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Muhammad holds that if the husband apostatizes, it is talaq by analogy with i‘bā’. Abu Yusuf originally held a similar view for i‘bā’ but agrees with Abu Hanifa on apostasy.
The text says 'hādhā bi-iṭlāqihi 'inda al-shaykhayn' – the relied-upon view is immediate faskh.
4
Apply the mahr rules to four scenarios
Work through the four possibilities: (1) Husband apostatizes before consummation – half mahr. (2) Husband after – full mahr. (3) Wife after – full mahr. (4) Wife before – no mahr.
The passage explicitly lists these scenarios.
5
Analyze the exceptional case of joint apostasy
If both apostatize and return to Islam simultaneously, the marriage remains intact by istihsan, based on the Banu Hanifa incident and Companion consensus.
In analogy (Zufar's view), separation occurs, but istihsan prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فسخ في الحال: [سواء كان قبل الدخول أو بعده (ط)] أي إذا ارتد أحد الزوجين عن الإسلام فسخ النكاح في الحال أي فرق بينهما بغير طلاق، وهذا بإطلاقه عند الشيخين، وقال محمد: إن كانت الردة من الزوج فهي فرقة بطلاق اعتباراً بالإباء، وأبو يوسف مر على أصله الذي كان له في الإباء، وأبو حنيفة فرق بأن الردة منافية للنكاح لكونها منافية للعصمة، والطلاق رافع للنكاح فتعذر أن يجعل الردة طلاقاً، بخلاف الإباء؛ لأنه يفوت الإمساك بالمعروف فيجب التسريح بالإحسان، ولهذا يتوقف الفرقة بالإباء على القضاء، ولا يتوقف بالردة، وقال الشافعي: إن كانت الردة بعد الدخول لا تبين منه حتى تمضي ثلاثة قروء، وإن كانت قبل الدخول تبين في الحال. (عيني، مستخلص) كلها سواء كانت الردة منها أو منه؛ لأنه تأكد بالدخول فلا يتصور سقوطه. (ع) إن ارتد: [ أي الزوج؛ لأن الفرقة من جهته قبل الدخول فوجب نصف المهر . اعلم أن الردة لا يخلو إما أن يكون قبل الدخول أو بعده، فإن كان بعد الدخول فلا تخلو أيضاً إما أن يكون المرتد هو الزوج أو المرأة، فحصل أربع صور: أحدها: أن يرتد الزوج قبل الدخول فللمرأة نصف المهر، وثانيها: أن يرتد هو بعد الدخول فلها تمام المهر، والثالث: أن ترتد المرأة بعد الدخول فلها تمام المهر أيضاً، والرابع أن ترتد المرأة قبل الدخول فلا مهر لها وهو معنى قوله: "وإن ارتدت لا"، وكذا لا نفقة لها؛ لأن الفرقة من قبلها، وفي ارتداد الزوج الفرقة من قبله فاعتبر بتطليقه. (مستخلص) وإن ارتدت لا: أي المرأة قبل الدخول لا يجب لها شيء؛ لأن الفرقة من جهتها (ع) والإباء: أي إباء أحدهما عن الإسلام بعد إسلام الآخر. (ع) نظيره أي نظير الارتداد حتى إذا كان بعد الدخول من أيهما كان يجب المهر كله وإن كان قبل الدخول فإن كان منه يجب النصف، وإن كان منها لا يجب شيء. (ع) لم تبن: أي إذا ارتد الزوجان معاً ثم أسلما معاً ولم يعرف سبق أحدهما على الآخر في الارتداد والإسلام لم تبن المرأة فهما على نكاحهما استحساناً، وفي القياس تقع الفرقة بينهما وهو قول زفر؛ لأن ردة أحدهما منافية فردتهما بالأولى؛ لأن في ردتهما ردة أحدهما وجه الاستحسان: أن بني حنيفة ارتدوا ثم أسلموا ولم تأمرهم الصحابة بتجديد الأنكحة، وكان هذا في زمن خلافة أبي بكر له. فإن قيل: إن ارتدادهم ما وقع جملة بالإجماع فكيف يستقيم الاستدلال به؟ قلنا عند جهالة التاريخ يجعل في حكم كأنه وجد جملة أي ارتدادهم وإسلامهم وقع معاً؛ لجهالة التاريخ، فترك القياس لإجماع الصحابة. (مستخلص عيني، فتح) لو أسلما: أي المرتدان حال كون كلّ واحد منهما متعاقباً للآخر. (ط) متعاقباً: لأن إسلام أحدهما إذا تقدم وبقي الآخر على ردته فيتحقق الاختلاف، وإن كان المتأخر إسلاماً هي المرأة قبل الدخول سقط المهر، وإن كان هو الزوج لها نصف المهر أو المتعة
Dissolution immediately: [whether before or after consummation (T)] i.e., if one of the two spouses apostatizes from Islam, the marriage is dissolved immediately, i.e., they are separated without divorce. This is absolute according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Muhammad said: If apostasy is from the husband, it is a separation by divorce, by analogy with i‘bā’ (refusal to convert). Abu Yusuf followed his original position regarding i‘bā’. Abu Hanifa differentiated: apostasy contradicts marriage because it contradicts the sanctity (ʿisma), and divorce removes the marriage, so it is impossible to make apostasy a divorce, unlike i‘bā’ because it causes failure of retention with kindness, so release with kindness becomes obligatory. Therefore, separation due to i‘bā’ depends on a judicial ruling, while separation due to apostasy does not. Al-Shafi‘i said: If apostasy is after consummation, she does not become separate until three menstrual periods pass; if before consummation, she becomes separate immediately. (Ayni, Mustakhlas) All of it whether apostasy is from her or from him because it is confirmed by consummation, so its fall cannot be imagined. (A) 'If he apostatizes': i.e., the husband, because separation is from his side before consummation, so half the dower is obligatory. Know that apostasy is either before consummation or after. If after consummation, then also either the apostate is the husband or the wife, so there are four scenarios: First: the husband apostatizes before consummation – for her half the dower. Second: he apostatizes after consummation – for her the entire dower. Third: the wife apostatizes after consummation – for her also the entire dower. Fourth: the wife apostatizes before consummation – no dower for her, and that is the meaning of His saying: 'And if she apostatizes, no.' Similarly, no maintenance for her because the separation is from her side. In the husband's apostasy, the separation is from his side, so it is considered like his divorce. (Mustakhlas) 'And if she apostatizes, no': i.e., the woman before consummation, nothing is obligatory for her because the separation is from her side. (A) 'And i‘bā’': i.e., the refusal of one of them to accept Islam after the other's conversion. (A) 'Its analogy': i.e., analogous to apostasy, so that if it is after consummation from either party, the entire dower is obligatory; if before consummation, if it is from him, half is obligatory; if from her, nothing is obligatory. (A) 'She does not separate': i.e., if both spouses apostatize together then embrace Islam together, and priority of one over the other in apostasy and Islam is not known, the woman is not separated; they remain in their marriage by istihsan. In analogy, separation occurs between them, which is the view of Zufar, because the apostasy of one is contradictory, so their apostasy is even more so. The reason for istihsan: The Banu Hanifa apostatized then embraced Islam, and the Companions did not order them to renew their marriages. This was during the caliphate of Abu Bakr. If it is said: Their apostasy did not occur as a single group according to consensus, so how can it be used as evidence? We say: When the date is unknown, it is considered as if it occurred simultaneously. Consequently, their apostasy and Islam occurred together due to ignorance of the date, so analogy was abandoned due to consensus of the Companions. (Mustakhlas, Ayni, Fath) 'If they embrace Islam': i.e., the two apostates while each one is consecutive to the other. (T) 'Consecutively': because if one's Islam precedes and the other remains in his apostasy, then difference is realized. And if the later one to Islam is the woman before consummation, the dower falls; if it is the husband, for her half the dower or mut‘a.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling on apostasy (riddah) and its effect on marriage. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs), apostasy by either spouse immediately dissolves the marriage without divorce (faskh). Muhammad al-Shaybani distinguishes: if the husband apostatizes, it is a divorce (talaq), by analogy with i‘bā’ (a spouse's refusal to embrace Islam when the other converts). Abu Hanifa rejects that analogy because apostasy contradicts the very basis of marriage (ʿisma), while i‘bā’ merely prevents a harmonious marriage; thus, apostasy causes immediate separation while i‘bā’ requires a judicial order. The mahr consequences depend on four scenarios: (1) Husband apostatizes before consummation – half mahr. (2) Husband apostatizes after consummation – full mahr. (3) Wife apostatizes after consummation – full mahr. (4) Wife apostatizes before consummation – no mahr. The passage also addresses the case where both spouses apostatize together and then return to Islam simultaneously: by istihsan, the marriage remains intact, based on the practice of Banu Hanifa who apostatized en masse and later returned to Islam without renewing marriages. If they embrace Islam consecutively (one after another), separation occurs because there is a period where one is Muslim and the other is not. The mahr in that case depends on who converted first and the timing.

Key Terms
الردة (al-riddah): Apostasy: renunciation of Islam by a Muslim; in this context, it triggers immediate dissolution of marriage.
الإباء (al-ibā’): Refusal: when one spouse converts to Islam and the other refuses to convert, leading to judicial separation.
العصمة (al-ʿisma): Sanctity or inviolability of marriage; the bond that makes marriage legitimate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (Abdullah and Yusuf), if a wife apostatizes after consummation, what is the mahr entitlement?
A) Half the mahr
B) Full mahr
C) No mahr
D) Mut'a (compensation)

Key Takeaway
Apostasy immediately dissolves the marriage (faskh), and the mahr depends on which spouse apostatizes and whether consummation occurred, with the wife forfeiting mahr only if she apostatizes before consummation.

Nikah — Marriage
