Christian Woman under Muslim Husband Becomes Magian
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dual conversion to Magianism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on marriage separation when both spouses become Magian, and explain the legal basis for each.

Core Idea
When both spouses become Magian, Abu Yusuf sees it as the husband's apostasy alone; Muhammad sees it as mutual apostasy.

This passage presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement about a specific marital scenario: a Christian woman married to a Muslim, and both convert to Magianism (Zoroastrianism). The ruling on the validity of the marriage depends on whether the husband's conversion is considered his sole apostasy or whether both spouses have jointly apostatized. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani derive different conclusions from the same facts, and their disagreement illustrates deeper principles about how Hanafi jurists classify disbelief and its effect on marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and legal question
The case: a Christian woman is married to a Muslim husband. Both convert to Magianism. The legal question: does this cause the marriage to dissolve (separate) or does it remain intact?
Magianism is not a protected religion (ahl al-kitab), so it affects the validity of the marriage.
2
State Abu Yusuf's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf rules that separation occurs. His reasoning: the husband cannot remain a Magian; he must be compelled to Islam, so it is effectively his apostasy alone that dissolves the marriage. Moreover, all disbelief is one religion, so moving from Christianity to Magianism is not a new conversion but just a shift within disbelief. He analogizes to both becoming Jews, which also dissolves the marriage by consensus.
Key principle: 'al-kufr kulluhu millatun wahida' – all disbelief is one religion.
3
State Muhammad's position and reasoning
Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees. He argues that both have apostatized because converting from Christianity to Magianism is like initiating a fresh form of disbelief (since a Muslim cannot marry a Magian woman). Since they both performed the act together, it is mutual apostasy, not solely the husband's. He distinguishes Magianism from Judaism: converting to Judaism does not have the same effect because Judaism is a protected religion.
Muhammad focuses on the nature of Magianism as a distinct, non-protected faith.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The practical consequence: according to Abu Yusuf, the marriage is dissolved immediately, and the spouses must separate. According to Muhammad, the marriage remains valid, and they may continue as husband and wife.
This difference affects the couple's daily life and future marital relations.
5
Evaluate the strength of each argument
Abu Yusuf's argument relies on the maxim that all disbelief is one, and on the fact that the husband is compelled to Islam. Muhammad's argument emphasizes the distinct status of Magianism and the symmetry of both conversions. The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi (relied upon for fatwa), but later Hanafi works often favor Abu Yusuf in such cases.
Understanding both arguments deepens one's grasp of Hanafi usul and the boundaries of ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كانت نصرانية تحت مسلم فتمجسا وقعت الفرقة بينهما عند أبي يوسف؛ لأن الزوج لا يقرّ على ذلك الدين بل يجبر على الإسلام والمرأة تقر عليه، فصار كردّة الزوج وحده لما عرف ف أن الكفر كله ملة واحدة فالانتقال من كفر إلى كفر لا يجعل كالإنشاء فصار كما لو تهودا فإن الفرقة تقع بالاتفاق، فكذا هذا خلافاً لمحمد شه رمز الحقائق ٢٢٦/١٢] لأنهما ارتدا معا؛ لأن تمجس النصرانية كإحداث أصل الكفر؛ لأن المجوسية لا يجوز للمسلم أن يتزوج بها فإحداثها كإحداث الردة بخلاف اليهودية، ألا ترى أنها لو تمجست وحدها تقع الفرقة بينهما ولو تعودت لا تقع، فافترقا. (فتح)
And if a Christian woman was under a Muslim husband and both become Magians, separation occurs between them according to Abu Yusuf, because the husband is not allowed to remain upon that religion; rather he is compelled to Islam, while the woman is allowed to remain upon it. So it is like the husband's apostasy alone, because it is known that all disbelief is one religion, so moving from one disbelief to another is not like initiating (apostasy). So it is as if they both become Jews, for separation occurs by agreement. So likewise this, contrary to Muhammad. [Reference] because they both apostatized together, for a Christian becoming Magian is like introducing the basis of disbelief, because a Muslim is not permitted to marry a Magian woman, so her doing so is like initiating apostasy, unlike Judaism. Do you not see that if she alone becomes Magian, separation occurs between them, but if she returns (to Christianity), it does not occur? So they differ.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage deals with a specific scenario: a Christian woman married to a Muslim, and both the husband and wife convert to Magianism (Zoroastrianism). According to Abu Yusuf, separation occurs immediately. His reasoning: the husband is not allowed to remain on that religion; he must be compelled to embrace Islam, while the wife is allowed to remain as a Magian. Therefore, it is essentially the husband's apostasy that causes separation. Furthermore, since all forms of disbelief are considered one religion (kulluhu millatun wāḥida), moving from Christianity to Magianism is not considered a new conversion but a continuation of disbelief. Thus, the situation is analogous to both converting to Judaism, in which separation occurs by consensus. Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees: he argues that both have apostatized because converting from Christianity to Magianism is like introducing a new form of disbelief (iḥdāth aṣl al-kufr). Since a Muslim cannot marry a Magian woman, her conversion is like her apostasy. Muhammad distinguishes Magianism from Judaism: if a Christian woman converts to Judaism, she is still ahl al-kitāb? Actually, the text says that converting from Christianity to Judaism does not cause the same effect because Judaism is also a protected religion? The commentary explains that if she alone becomes Magian, separation occurs, but if she returns to Christianity, no separation. So the two imams differ on the nature of the conversion.

Key Terms
التمجس (al-tamajjus): Becoming Magian (Zoroastrian).
اليهودية (al-yahūdiyya): Judaism; conversion to Judaism from Christianity is considered a change within ahl al-kitab, not apostasy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, why does the marriage dissolve when a Christian woman and her Muslim husband both become Magians?
A) Because both have apostatized from Islam.
B) Because the husband cannot remain a Magian and is compelled to Islam, making it like his apostasy alone.
C) Because Magianism is considered worse than Christianity.
D) Because the wife's conversion alone would dissolve the marriage even without the husband.

Key Takeaway
When both spouses convert to Magianism, Abu Yusuf rules separation due to the husband's effective apostasy, while Muhammad rules no separation considering joint conversion, illustrating the Hanafi ikhtilaf on the nature of disbelief.

Mahr for the Apostate Spouse: The Four Cases and the Analogy to Talaq
Ruling on dower in four cases of apostasy with analogy to refusal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mahr in each of the four apostasy scenarios and explain the qiyas to talaq.

Core Idea
Half mahr for husband's apostasy, none for wife's before consummation; full mahr after, regardless of who apostatized.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents the rulings on mahr when apostasy occurs before or after consummation. The commentator expands on the concise matn by enumerating all four possible cases and drawing an analogy to divorce (talaq). Understanding these rulings requires grasping the principle that the party causing the separation bears the financial consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The dower (mahr) in cases of apostasy. State the general principle: after consummation full dower is due regardless; before consummation, it depends on who apostatized.
The matn uses very concise language; the commentary unpacks it.
2
Distinguish the four cases
List the four scenarios based on timing (before/after consummation) and the apostate (husband/wife). For each, state the mahr ruling.
Memorize this breakdown: before consummation: husband=half, wife=none; after consummation: both=full.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to talaq before consummation. The commentator explains that apostasy is not itself a divorce but the separation is caused by the apostate, similar to a husband's talaq.
The statement: 'وَالطَّلَاقُ رَافِعٌ لِلنِّكَاحِ فَتَعَذَّرَ أَنْ يُجْعَلَ الرِّدَّةُ طَلَاقًا' clarifies the analogy.
4
Understand the analogy (qiyas)
Identify the original case (asl): talaq before consummation (half mahr). The new case (far'): apostasy before consummation. The effective cause (illa): the dissolution initiated by one spouse. The ruling transfers: half mahr. After consummation, the analogy does not apply because the right is confirmed.
The illa is that the party causing the separation forfeits the right to avoid full mahr.
5
Note the difference with iba'
Iba' (refusal to embrace Islam after the spouse's conversion) is analogous to apostasy in mahr ruling but differs in that it requires a judicial decree (qada) while apostasy does not.
Iba' does not automatically dissolve the marriage; only a qadi can separate them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فللموطوءة المهرُ كلُّها، ولغيرها نصفه إن ارتد، وإن ارتدت لا، والإباء نظيره. أي غير الموطوءة المهر الزوج. أي المرأة أي لا يجب لها شيء للعصمة، والطلاق رافع للنكاح فتعذر أن يجعل الردة طلاقاً، بخلاف الإباء؛ لأنه يفوت الإمساك بالمعروف فيجب التسريح بالإحسان، ولهذا يتوقف الفرقة بالإباء على القضاء، ولا يتوقف بالردة. (عيني، مستخلص) كلها سواء كانت الردة منها أو منه؛ لأنه تأكد بالدخول فلا يتصور سقوطه. (ع) إن ارتد: أي الزوج؛ لأن الفرقة من جهته قبل الدخول فوجب نصف المهر . اعلم أن الردة لا يخلو إما أن يكون قبل الدخول أو بعده، فإن كان بعد الدخول فلا تخلو أيضاً إما أن يكون المرتد هو الزوج أو المرأة، فحصل أربع صور: أحدها: أن يرتد الزوج قبل الدخول فللمرأة نصف المهر، وثانيها: أن يرتد هو بعد الدخول فلها تمام المهر، والثالث: أن ترتد المرأة بعد الدخول فلها تمام المهر أيضاً، والرابع أن ترتد المرأة قبل الدخول فلا مهر لها وهو معنى قوله: "وإن ارتدت لا"، وكذا لا نفقة لها؛ لأن الفرقة من قبلها، وفي ارتداد الزوج الفرقة من قبله فاعتبر بتطليقه. (مستخلص) وإن ارتدت لا: أي المرأة قبل الدخول لا يجب لها شيء؛ لأن الفرقة من جهتها (ع) والإباء: أي إباء أحدهما عن الإسلام بعد إسلام الآخر. (ع) نظيره أي نظير الارتداد حتى إذا كان بعد الدخول من أيهما كان يجب المهر كله وإن كان قبل الدخول فإن كان منه يجب النصف، وإن كان منها لا يجب شيء. (ع)
For the woman who has been consummated, the entire dower is due; for other than her, half of it if he apostatizes, but if she apostatizes then nothing; and refusal (to embrace Islam) is analogous to it. (Commentary): ... the four cases: first, if the husband apostatizes before consummation, she gets half the dower; second, if he apostatizes after consummation, she gets the full dower; third, if she apostatizes after consummation, she also gets the full dower; fourth, if she apostatizes before consummation, she gets no dower. Similarly, for refusal, same ruling as apostasy.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on dower (mahr) when apostasy or refusal (al-iba') occurs in a marriage. The source text is an expansion on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author divides the case into four scenarios based on whether the apostasy happened before or after consummation and whether the husband or wife apostatized. After consummation, the dower is fully due regardless of who apostatized, because the marriage was confirmed. Before consummation, if the husband apostatizes, the wife gets half the dower (as in talaq before consummation); if the wife apostatizes, she gets nothing because she caused the separation. The rule for 'iba' (when one spouse refuses to embrace Islam after the other has converted) is identical to apostasy. The commentator notes that the separation due to apostasy does not require a judicial decree, unlike refusal which requires a qadi's ruling. This is because apostasy automatically dissolves the marriage, whereas refusal only makes the marriage impossible to continue in a good manner, so the husband must release the wife with kindness. The four-case summary is provided to clarify the implications of the concise matn.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The dower or bridal gift that is the wife's exclusive property, determined by the contract or subsequent consummation.
ردة (ridda): Apostasy from Islam by word or deed; it dissolves the marriage immediately.
إباء (iba'): Refusal of a spouse of the People of the Book to embrace Islam after their spouse has converted, leading to judicial separation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a wife apostatizes before consummation, how much dower does she receive?
A) Full dower
B) Half dower
C) One-third dower
D) No dower

Key Takeaway
The dower in apostasy cases depends on who caused the separation and whether consummation occurred; the husband's apostasy before consummation triggers half mahr, while the wife's apostasy before consummation triggers none.

When Apostasy Dissolves Marriage: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on apostasy dissolution timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on when apostasy dissolves a marriage and explain the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence of the difference).

Core Idea
Hanafi: immediate dissolution; Shafi'i: delay after consummation until three menstrual cycles pass.

Apostasy (riddah) by a spouse raises the question: when exactly does the marriage end? The Hanafi school holds that apostasy severs the marriage instantly, whether before or after consummation. Imam al-Shafi'i distinguished: before consummation it is immediate, but after consummation the marriage persists until three menstrual periods (quru') have elapsed. This lesson unpacks the Shafi'i argument and the Hanafi rationale, and highlights the practical consequence for couples reconciling after apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The legal issue is the timing of marital dissolution (firqah) due to apostasy. Does it occur instantly or after a waiting period? The answer differs based on whether the marriage was consummated.
This is a classic inter-madhhab ikhtilaf with practical implications.
2
State the Hanafi position
Recall the Hanafi ruling as implied in earlier lessons: apostasy dissolves the marriage immediately, both before and after consummation. The evidence includes the Quranic prohibition of a Muslim woman staying married to a non-Muslim.
The Hanafi view relies on the principle that apostasy nullifies the marriage contract itself.
3
State the Shafi'i position
The source text quotes Imam al-Shafi'i: if apostasy occurs after consummation, the marriage does not dissolve until three menstrual cycles (quru') pass; if before consummation, it dissolves immediately.
The Shafi'i reasoning is based on analogy (qiyas) to the 'idda of divorce, viewing the post-consummation bond as stronger.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Shafi'i
Hanafis respond that apostasy is not a form of divorce but a complete nullification of the marriage tie. Therefore, no 'idda is needed for dissolution; the 'idda for remarriage, if needed, is separate.
This is a fundamental disagreement about the nature of the dissolution – fasikh (rescission) versus talaq (divorce).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: For Hanafis, if the apostate returns to Islam, a new marriage contract is required to reunite. For Shafi'is, if the husband returns within three menstrual cycles, the marriage revives without a new contract.
This difference affects legal advice given to couples in such circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشافعي: إن كانت الردة بعد الدخول لا تبين منه حتى تمضي ثلاثة قروء، وإن كانت قبل الدخول تبين في الحال. (عيني، مستخلص)
And al-Shafi'i said: If the apostasy is after consummation, she does not separate from him until three menstrual periods pass; and if it is before consummation, she separates immediately.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage presents the Shafi'i position on the timing of marital dissolution due to apostasy. The Hanafi school holds that apostasy dissolves the marriage instantly both before and after consummation (as implied in the previous lesson's ruling on mahr). Imam al-Shafi'i distinguishes: before consummation, dissolution is immediate; after consummation, the marriage remains until the expiry of three menstrual cycles (quru'). The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that during that period, the husband might return to Islam and the marriage would be revived without a new contract according to Shafi'is, whereas Hanafis require a new contract if they reconcile after apostasy. The Hanafi response to this is not detailed here but is based on the principle that apostasy is a complete negation of the marriage bond, and the evidence from the general rule that a Muslim woman cannot remain married to a non-Muslim.

Key Terms
قروء (quru'): Menstrual cycles; the 'idda period for a woman after divorce or dissolution.
فرقة (firqah): Separation; dissolution of the marriage tie.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Shafi'i, if a husband apostatizes after consummation, when does the marriage dissolve?
A) Immediately
B) After three menstrual periods
C) After one month
D) Only if he does not return to Islam

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school dissolves marriage immediately upon apostasy; the Shafi'i school delays it after consummation until three menstrual cycles pass, a difference rooted in their understanding of the nature of dissolution.

Simultaneous Apostasy and Return: Marriage Continuity
Ruling on marriage continuity when both spouses apostatize and return together

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage continuity when both spouses apostatize and return together, explain Zufar's opposing view, and evaluate the istihsan based on Companions' consensus.

Core Idea
When both apostatize and return together, uncertainty preserves the marriage.

Apostasy generally severs the marital tie, but what if both spouses engage in it simultaneously? The Hanafi school navigates a subtle intra-madhhab dispute, balancing strict analogy (qiyas) with a key historical precedent from the Companions. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the majority view that the marriage remains valid when timing is unknown.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling when both spouses apostatize together and return together without knowing who preceded. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold the marriage remains valid.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize Zufar's view: he argues that apostasy by either spouse dissolves the marriage, so apostasy by both does so even more strongly. His method is strict qiyas.
Zufar is a respected Hanafi scholar, showing legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand Zufar's qiyas: the marriage is inconsistent with apostasy in one spouse; therefore, both apostatizing should be more severe and cause dissolution.
This seems logically consistent at first glance.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The majority invoke istihsan based on the Companions' treatment of Banu Hanifa. Since the order of apostasy and return is unknown, it is treated as if both occurred simultaneously, preserving the marriage.
The key is uncertainty (jahalah) in timing, making the qiyas inapplicable.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the majority view, no new nikah is needed; under Zufar's view, a new contract would be required if the couple wishes to remain married.
This difference affects real cases where timing is unclear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم تبن: أي إذا ارتد الزوجان معاً ثم أسلما معاً ولم يعرف سبق أحدهما على الآخر في الارتداد والإسلام لم تبن المرأة فهما على نكاحهما استحساناً، وفي القياس تقع الفرقة بينهما وهو قول زفر؛ لأن ردة أحدهما منافية فردتهما بالأولى؛ لأن في ردتهما ردة أحدهما وجه الاستحسان: أن بني حنيفة ارتدوا ثم أسلموا ولم تأمرهم الصحابة بتجديد الأنكحة، وكان هذا في زمن خلافة أبي بكر له. فإن قيل: إن ارتدادهم ما وقع جملة بالإجماع فكيف يستقيم الاستدلال به؟ قلنا عند جهالة التاريخ يجعل في حكم كأنه وجد جملة أي ارتدادهم وإسلامهم وقع معاً؛ لجهالة التاريخ، فترك القياس لإجماع الصحابة. (مستخلص عيني، فتح)
She does not separate: i.e., if both spouses apostatize together then both embrace Islam together, and it is not known which of them preceded the other in apostasy and Islam, the wife does not separate; they remain in their marriage by istihsan. In qiyas, separation would occur, and this is the view of Zufar, because the apostasy of one is inconsistent with marriage, so the apostasy of both is even more so. The basis of istihsan: the Banu Hanifa apostatized then embraced Islam, and the Companions did not order them to renew their marriages, and this was during the caliphate of Abu Bakr. If it is said: their apostasy did not occur all at once by consensus, so how can this be used as evidence? We say: when the date is unknown, it is considered as if the apostasy and Islam occurred together; due to ignorance of the date, qiyas is abandoned in favor of the consensus of the Companions.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage discusses the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of simultaneous apostasy and subsequent simultaneous return to Islam. The main position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad) is that if both spouses apostatize and then both return to Islam, and it is unknown who apostatized first, the marriage remains valid. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) supported by the example of the Banu Hanifa tribe, who all apostatized during the Caliphate of Abu Bakr and then returned to Islam. The Companions did not require them to renew their marriages, indicating that when the timing is uncertain, the marriage is considered continuous. Zufar disagreed, arguing that apostasy by either spouse dissolves the marriage, so both apostatizing does even more. However, the majority in the Hanafi school rejected this qiyas because of the uncertainty, and accepted the practice of the Companions as implicit ijma (consensus). Thus, the mufta bihi position is that the marriage is not dissolved in such a case.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that abandons strict analogy due to a stronger evidence (here, the practice of the Companions).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if both spouses apostatize and then both return to Islam, and it is unknown who was first, what is the status of the marriage?
A) It is automatically dissolved
B) It remains valid
C) It depends on the imam's decision
D) It is suspended until one of them converts

Key Takeaway
When both spouses apostatize and return together with unknown timing, the marriage remains valid by istihsan, based on the Companions' consensus.

Sequential Apostasy and Spousal Conversion to Magianism
Ruling on sequential return to Islam and conversion to Magianism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the effect of a Muslim husband and his Christian wife both converting to Magianism, and identify the basis of their qiyas.

Core Idea
When both spouses convert to Magianism, Abu Yusuf dissolves the marriage; Muhammad keeps it intact—all because of how they analogize to Judaism vs. apostasy.

This passage from Fath al-Qadir presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute. After clarifying the rules of sequential apostasy and dower, the text addresses a subtle case: a Muslim man with a Christian wife and both convert to Magianism. Imam Abu Yusuf rules that the marriage dissolves by analogy to conversion to Judaism, while Imam Muhammad argues that Magianism is like apostasy and simultaneous conversion does not dissolve it. Understanding their reasoning sharpens our grasp of qiyas and the concept of 'one religion of disbelief.'

Guided Path
1
Understand sequential apostasy rule
When apostate spouses return to Islam sequentially, the marriage's fate depends on who returns first. If the wife returns first before consummation, the dower falls; if the husband returns first, she gets half dower or mut'ah.
This rule is tied to the concept of difference (ikhtilaf) in religion at the time of return.
2
Identify the core case
A Muslim husband with a Christian wife both convert to Magianism (Zoroastrianism). Does the marriage dissolve? Two Hanafi masters differ.
Magianism is considered a religion with which Muslim marriage is not permissible.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and qiyas
Abu Yusuf says the marriage dissolves. He analogizes to a Muslim couple both converting to Judaism, which by consensus dissolves the marriage. His reasoning: the husband cannot remain Magian and is compelled to Islam, while the wife can remain, so it is like his apostasy alone.
He relies on the principle that 'all disbelief is one religion,' so moving from one kufr to another is not like initiating a new religion.
4
State Muhammad's position and qiyas
Muhammad says the marriage does not dissolve. He argues that Magianism is different from Judaism: a Muslim may marry a Jewess but not a Magian woman. Therefore, converting to Magianism is like initiating disbelief (apostasy). Since both did it together, it resembles simultaneous apostasy with unknown order, which does not dissolve the marriage.
He points out that if the wife alone becomes Magian, separation occurs; but if both do so together, the order is unknown, so the marriage remains.
5
Identify thamarah al-khilaf and note mufta bihi
The practical difference: if the husband alone converts, dissolution is certain. When both convert, Abu Yusuf still dissolves; Muhammad keeps it intact. Later Hanafi works may follow Abu Yusuf for strictness, but the text leaves it unsettled.
The mufta bihi is not explicitly stated here; the dispute remains within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو أسلما: أي المرتدان حال كون كلّ واحد منهما متعاقباً للآخر. (ط) متعاقباً: لأن إسلام أحدهما إذا تقدم وبقي الآخر على ردته فيتحقق الاختلاف، وإن كان المتأخر إسلاماً هي المرأة قبل الدخول سقط المهر، وإن كان هو الزوج لها نصف المهر أو المتعة ولو كانت نصرانية تحت مسلم فتمجسا وقعت الفرقة بينهما عند أبي يوسف؛ لأن الزوج لا يقرّ على ذلك الدين بل يجبر على الإسلام والمرأة تقر عليه، فصار كردّة الزوج وحده لما عرف ف أن الكفر كله ملة واحدة فالانتقال من كفر إلى كفر لا يجعل كالإنشاء فصار كما لو تهودا فإن الفرقة تقع بالاتفاق، فكذا هذا خلافاً لمحمد شه رمز الحقائق ٢٢٦/١٢] لأنهما ارتدا معا؛ لأن تمجس النصرانية كإحداث أصل الكفر؛ لأن المجوسية لا يجوز للمسلم أن يتزوج بها فإحداثها كإحداث الردة بخلاف اليهودية، ألا ترى أنها لو تمجست وحدها تقع الفرقة بينهما ولو تعودت لا تقع، فافترقا. (فتح)
If they both embrace Islam: i.e., the two apostates while each is succeeding the other. (T) Succeeding: because if one's Islam precedes and the other remains in apostasy, difference is confirmed; if the one later in Islam is the wife before consummation, the dower falls; if it is the husband, she gets half the dower or mut'ah. And if a Christian woman was under a Muslim, and they both become Magian, separation occurs between them according to Abu Yusuf, because the husband is not allowed to remain in that religion but is compelled to Islam, while the wife can remain, so it becomes like the apostasy of the husband alone. Since it is established that all disbelief is one religion, moving from one disbelief to another is not considered like initiating it; so it is as if they both became Jews, in which case separation occurs by consensus. This is contrary to Muhammad's view, because they both apostatized together; for a Christian turning Magian is like initiating disbelief, since a Muslim cannot marry a Magian woman, so it is like initiating apostasy, unlike Judaism. Do you not see that if she alone becomes Magian, separation occurs, but if she reverts, it does not; so they differ.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage deals with two scenarios: first, sequential return to Islam by apostate spouses. If the wife returns first and the husband remains apostate, the marriage ends; the dower depends on consummation timing. Second, a more specific intra-Hanafi dispute: if a Muslim husband and his Christian wife both become Magian (Zoroastrian), does the marriage dissolve? Abu Yusuf says yes, by analogy to both converting to Judaism, which dissolves the marriage by consensus. His reasoning: the husband cannot remain a Magian; he must be compelled to Islam; the wife may remain. This is treated as the husband's apostasy alone. Muhammad disagrees: turning from Christianity to Magianism is a complete change of religion because marriage to a Magian is prohibited for a Muslim; thus it is like both apostatizing, but he holds that since they both changed together, the marriage is not dissolved? Actually the text says 'because they both apostatized together' but then contradicts that? Let's read carefully: The text says 'خلافاً لمحمد' so Muhammad holds that separation does not occur? But the phrase 'لأنهما ارتدا معا' suggests Muhammad's view is that since they both apostatized together, it is like simultaneous apostasy, and as per the previous lesson, simultaneous apostasy with unknown order does not dissolve the marriage. However, the text then says 'فافترقا' meaning Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ. So the mufta bihi is not explicitly stated here; the commentator (Fath) notes the difference. The practical implication: if a Christian wife of a Muslim becomes Magian, does the marriage end immediately? According to Abu Yusuf yes, according to Muhammad it remains until they both return? The text suggests that if she alone becomes Magian, separation occurs; if both do, the dispute arises. This is a nuanced intra-madhhab difference.

Key Terms
تمجس (tamajjus): Conversion to Magianism (Zoroastrianism).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis of Muhammad's disagreement with Abu Yusuf regarding both spouses converting to Magianism?
A) Muhammad believes the husband cannot be compelled to Islam
B) Muhammad argues that Magianism is like apostasy, not a mere change within kufr
C) Muhammad relies on a hadith that says marriage survives apostasy
D) Muhammad follows the earlier rule of sequential apostasy

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's dispute on conversion to Magianism highlights how the marriageability of the new religion affects whether it is treated as a change within disbelief or as apostasy.

Definition and Obligation of Qasm (Division Among Wives)
Introduction to the chapter on division of time among wives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qasm technically and state its obligation with its Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Justice among wives is wajib — qasm ensures equal nights, maintenance, and shelter, but not intercourse.

This opening passage of the chapter on qasm establishes the technical meaning of 'division' and its legal basis. It clarifies what is included (overnight stays, maintenance) and excluded (intercourse), and derives the obligation from the Quranic conditional permission for polygamy. Understanding this foundation is essential before studying the details of enforcement, waivers, and travel rulings.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic vs. technical definition
Note that qasm linguistically means apportioning shares, but technically it refers to equal treatment among wives in specific matters. The passage explicitly lists what is included and excluded.
Intercourse is excluded because it depends on inclination.
2
Identify the components of qasm
List the elements that are equalised: overnight stay (baytuta), maintenance (nafaqa), housing (sakan), food, and drink. Excluding intercourse is a key point.
3
Locate the dalil and its implication
The Quranic verse (4:3) conditions permission of polygamy on justice. The passage argues that this condition implies the obligation of justice (qasm) when multiple wives exist.
The verse: فَإِنْ خِفْتُمْ أَلَّا تَعْدِلُوا فَوَاحِدَةً
4
State the hukm and its basis
The ruling is wujub (obligation) for the husband to practice qasm. This is derived from the conditional prohibition of injustice.
Other schools agree on obligation but may differ on specifics like the new wife's prerogative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القسم أي في بيان أحكامه. باب القسم: هو التسوية بين الزوجات في البيتوتة والنفقة والسكنى والمأكل والمشرب لا الجماع. (ط)] لما ذكر جواز نكاح أربعة من النسوة للحر وثنتين للعبد لم يكن بد من بيان القسم، وهو بفتح القاف وسكون السين مصدر بمعنى تعيين أنصباء الشركاء لغةً، وبالكسر بمعنى النصيب، ويقال: كلاهما بمعنى النصيب، ولكن بالفتح يستعمل في موضع خاص، بخلاف الكسر ، وشرعاً بالفتح: التسوية بين المنكوحات البيتوتة والنفقة والسكنى لا في المجامعة؛ لأنها تبتني على النشاط، فلا يقدر على التسوية فيها كالمحبة، فلو كان عمل الزوج ليلاً كالحارس قسم نهاراً كما ذكره الشافعية وهو حسن، والقسم واجب؛ لقوله تعالى بعد بيان حل الأربع: ﴿فَإِنْ خِفْتُمْ أَلَّا تَعْدِلُوا فَوَاحِدَةً أَوْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُكُمْ (النساء:۳)، فاستفدنا حل الأربع مقيداً بعدم خوف عدم العدل، وثبوت المنع عن أكثر من واحدة عند خوفه، فعلم إيجابه عند تعددهن. (عيني، فتح)
Chapter of Division: i.e., concerning its rulings. Division is equal treatment among wives in overnight stay, maintenance, housing, food, and drink, not in intercourse. ... The division is obligatory; because Allah said after stating the permissibility of four: 'But if you fear you will not be just, then [marry] one or what your right hands possess' (4:3). So we understand that permission of four is conditioned upon not fearing injustice, and prohibition of more than one when injustice is feared, thus its obligation is known when multiple wives exist.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on qasm (division of time and resources among wives). The author defines qasm linguistically and technically: it means equal distribution of nights, maintenance, housing, food, and drink, but excludes sexual intercourse because that depends on inclination and cannot be regulated equally. The obligation of qasm is derived from the Quranic verse (4:3), which warns that if a husband cannot be just, he should marry only one. This implies that when he has multiple wives, justice (i.e., qasm) is obligatory. The Hanafi school makes this a wajib duty; the husband must allocate equal turns to each wife unless she voluntarily waives her right. The commentator also notes that if the husband works at night, division may be by day as the Shafi'is mention, which is a good opinion.

Key Terms
قسم (qasm): Technical: equal distribution of nights, maintenance, and shelter among wives, excluding intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is included in the obligation of qasm (division) among wives according to the Hanafi school?
A) Overnight stays and maintenance, but not intercourse
B) Equal sexual relations
C) Only overnight stays
D) Only maintenance

Key Takeaway
Qasm is wajib and includes overnight stay, maintenance, housing, food, and drink, but not intercourse, based on Quran 4:3.

Equality in Division Among All Wives and the Shafi'i Exception
Inter-madhhab dispute on extra nights for a new wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that all wives are equal in division (qasm) and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument regarding extra nights for a new wife.

Core Idea
A new wife is like an old wife in qasm; extra nights are not given, opposing the Shafi'i view.

This passage presents a key inter-madhhab dispute on the rights of wives to equal time from their husband. While the Hanafi school insists on strict equality, the Shafi'i school (along with Maliki and Hanbali) grants a new wife extra nights. Understanding the proofs and reconciliation is crucial for a correct fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on equality
Identify the core Hanafi ruling: all wives, new or old, virgin or non-virgin, Muslim or Kitabiyyah, are equal in the division of nights (qasm).
The phrase 'الجديدة كالقديمة مطلقاً' is the key.
2
Identify the opposing Shafi'i position
Recognize that Imam Shafi'i (along with Malik and Ahmad) gives a new wife extra nights: 7 for virgin, 3 for non-virgin, not counted in the regular cycle.
3
Follow the Hanafi dalil (general hadith)
Examine the hadith 'Whoever has two wives and inclines to one...' which is general and prohibits any preference without exception.
This hadith is from Abu Dawud, Nasa'i, and Ibn Majah.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i dalil (hadith of Anas)
Review the hadith 'For the virgin, seven; for the non-virgin, three; then he returns to his family.' Understand its wording and chain.
This hadith is reported by al-Daraqutni.
5
Understand the Hanafi response
See how Hanafis reinterpret the hadith of Anas as initial precedence (starting with the new wife) and then giving the old wife the same number, or as a settlement (sulh). The old wife is more deserving because her estrangement is greater.
This is a classic case of ta'wil (interpretive reconciliation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجديدة كالقديمة مطلقاً سواء كانت الجديدة بكراً أو ثيباً وخصهما مع أن المريضة والرتقاء والقرناء والمراهقة والمجنونة كغيرها؛ لأنهما محل الخلاف والتسوية بينهما في القسم؛ لقوله عليه: "من كانت له امرأتان فمال إلى إحداهما يوم القيامة وشقه مائل" أي مفلوج، رواه أبو داود والنسائي وابن ماجه فهو مطلق لا فصل فيه بين الجديدة والقديمة والبكر والثيب والمسلمة والكتابية؛ ولأن القسم من حقوق النكاح، ولا تفاوت بينهما في ذلك. وقال الشافعي: يقيم عند البكر الجديدة سبعاً وعند الثيب الجديدة ثلاثاً، ولا يحتسب عليها بذلك؛ لما روي عن أنس له أنه قال: سمعت رسول الله له يقول: "للبكر سبع وللثيب ثلاث، ثم يعود إلى أهله" أخرجه الدار قطني، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، قلنا: المراد منه التفضيل بالبداءة للجديدة دون الزيادة بأن يبدأ بالجديدة فيبيت عندها سبعاً إن كانت بكراً أو ثلاثاً إن كانت ثيباً، ثم يبيت عند القديمة كذلك أي سبعاً أو ثلاثاً، أو هو محمول على الصلح؛ ولأن القديمة أولى بالتفضيل؛ لأن الوحشة في جانبها أكثر . (عيني، مستخلص، فتح) فيه: أي في القسم، وكذلك الصحيحة والمريضة والرتقاء والمجنونة التي لا يخاف منها، والحائض والنفساء والحامل والحائل والصغيرة التي يمكن وطيها والمحرمة والمولى منها والمظاهر منها كلها سواء. (ط)
And the new wife is like the old wife absolutely, whether the new wife is a virgin or previously married, and specifically these two despite the fact that the sick, the woman with a vaginal obstruction, the deaf, the young, and the insane are like others; because these are the subject of disagreement. Equality between them in division is due to the saying of the Prophet: 'Whoever has two wives and inclines to one of them, on the Day of Resurrection he will be with one side inclined (paralyzed).' This hadith is general with no distinction between new and old, virgin and non-virgin, Muslim and Kitabiyyah. And because division is a right of marriage, there is no difference between them in that. Al-Shafi'i said: He stays with the new virgin for seven nights, and with the new non-virgin for three nights, and he does not count it against her; based on the hadith of Anas: 'For the virgin, seven; for the non-virgin, three; then he returns to his family.' We (the Hanafis) say: The meaning is preference in starting with the new wife, not extra; he begins with the new wife staying with her seven if virgin or three if non-virgin, then he stays with the old wife similarly, or it is interpreted as a settlement. Because the old wife is more deserving of preference because the estrangement is greater on her side.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage addresses a key inter-madhhab difference on division among wives. The Hanafi school holds that all wives are equal in the obligation of qasm, regardless of whether they are new or old, virgin or previously married, Muslim or Kitabiyyah. The proof is the general hadith promising punishment for inclining towards one wife. The Shafi'i school, following a separate hadith from Anas, gives the new wife extra nights: seven if virgin, three if previously married, and these extra nights are not counted as part of the regular cycle. The Hanafi response is that the hadith of Anas refers to the initial precedence (starting with the new wife) but after that, the old wife receives an equal number of consecutive nights to balance. Alternatively, it is interpreted as a temporary arrangement by mutual consent (sulh). The Hanafi view also emphasizes that the old wife is more in need of care because she feels more estranged. Thus, the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is complete equality. The passage also confirms that other conditions like illness, menses, pregnancy, etc., do not affect the right to a share.

Key Terms
قديمة (qadimah): Wife who has been in the marriage longer, as opposed to a newly married wife.
قسم (qasm): Division of nights among wives; the husband's duty to allocate equal time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many extra nights does a new wife who is a virgin receive?
A) Seven nights
B) Three nights
C) None; she is equal to the old wife
D) It depends on the husband's preference

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, all wives are equal in the division of nights; a new wife receives no extra nights, and the husband must maintain strict equality.

Division Between Free and Slave Wives
Ruling that free wife gets twice the share of a slave wife in division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding division of nights between a free wife and a slave wife, cite the proof from the Companions, and apply the rule to similar partial slave statuses.

Core Idea
Free wife gets two nights; slave wife gets one night—deficiency in status appears in the division.

In the division of nights among multiple wives, the Hanafi school makes a distinction based on freedom. This lesson examines the ruling that a free wife receives twice the share of a slave wife, the evidence from the sayings of the Companions, and how the rule extends to those with partial slave status such as mudabbar, mukataba, and umm walad. We also address the cross-madhhab disagreement with Imam Malik.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
State the ratio: free wife gets two nights, slave wife one. Note that this applies when a man has both a free and a slave wife.
2
Examine the textual proof
Read the saying of 'Ali (RA) and the judgment of Abu Bakr and 'Ali (RA). Understand that it is a qawl sahabi, which in Hanafi usul is a source.
3
Analyze the rationale (ta'lil)
The text gives the reason: the permissibility of a slave wife is less, as she cannot be married with a free woman. So the deficiency is shown in division.
4
Apply the extension to similar categories
The mudabbar, mukataba, and umm walad are treated like slave women because they still have some slavery.
5
Identify the ikhtilaf
Imam Malik holds they are equal. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: in practice, the Hanafi reduces the slave wife's nights, while the Maliki does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضعف الأمة: [والمدبر والمكاتبة، وعن مالك هما سواء (ع)] أي إن كان له امرأتان إحداهما حرة والأخرى أمة فللحرة ليلتان وللأمة ليلة من القسم؛ لقوله عليا للحرة ليلتان من القسم وللأمة ليلة"، وبذلك قضى الصديق وعلي ما؛ وهو لأن حل الأمة أنقص من حل الحرة بدليل أنه لا يجوز نكاحها معها ولا بعدها، فلا بد من إظهار النقصان في القسم، والمكاتبة والمدبرة وأم الولد بمنزلة الأمة؛ لأن الرق فيهن قائم سواء كن مسلمة أو ذمية والمريضة في القسم كالصحيحة، وكما أن مرضها لا يسقط حقها في القسم، فكذا مرضه لا يكون مسقطاً لحقهن فيه. (مستخلص، فتح)
Double that of the slave woman: (and the mudabbar and mukataba, and according to Malik they are equal). So if he has two wives, one free and one slave, the free wife has two nights and the slave wife has one night in the division; due to the saying of 'Ali: 'For the free woman, two nights, and for the slave woman, one night.' And by this did Abu Bakr and 'Ali judge. Because the permissibility of a slave is less than that of a free woman, as proven by the prohibition of marrying her alongside a free woman or after her, so the deficiency must be shown in the division. The mukataba, mudabbara, and umm walad are like the slave woman, because there remains slavery in them, whether Muslim or dhimmi. The sick wife is like the healthy in division; just as her illness does not remove her right to division, so his illness does not remove their rights.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage states a special rule: when a husband has both a free wife and a slave wife, the free wife receives two nights for every one night of the slave wife. This is based on a saying of 'Ali ibn Abi Talib and the practice of Abu Bakr and 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with them). The underlying reason is that the status of a slave wife is lower: a Muslim man may not marry a slave woman with a free woman (except under certain conditions) and if he does, the slave wife's share of nights is less. This deficiency in marital capacity is reflected in the division. The same rule applies to women who have a partial status of slavery: the mudabbara (promised freedom upon owner's death), the mukataba (slave under contract of manumission), and the umm walad (slave mother of the master's child) are all considered like slaves for this purpose, regardless of their religion. The passage also confirms that illness of either spouse does not affect the obligation; the healthy wife still has her right to a share of nights.

Key Terms
ضعف (dhi'f): Double; the free wife receives twice the share of the slave wife.
مدبرة (mudabbara): A slave woman who has been promised freedom upon the owner's death.
مكاتبة (mukataba): A slave woman under a contract of manumission, working to purchase her freedom.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a household with a free wife and a slave wife, the free wife receives how many nights compared to the slave wife?
A) Equal
B) Twice
C) Three times
D) None

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the division of nights favors a free wife over a slave wife in a 2:1 ratio, grounded in the practice of the early caliphs and extended to similar partial slave statuses.

beginner

The Obligation of Qasm and Equality in Division
Establishes the ruling and scope of qasm (division of nights)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of qasm, explain its obligation based on Quran 4:3, and identify the categories of wives that are treated equally in division.

Core Idea
Qasm is wajib for a husband with multiple wives; all free wives are equal in division.

This lesson defines qasm and establishes its obligatory nature. You will see how the Quran ties the permissibility of polygamy to justice, making fairness a binding duty. The Hanafi school then applies this equally to all free wives regardless of their status.

Guided Path
1
Define qasm linguistically and technically
Understand the difference between qasm and qism, and the scope of equality (overnight stay, maintenance, etc., not intercourse).
2
Identify the proof of obligation
Examine Quran 4:3: the permissibility of four wives is conditioned on justice; if you fear injustice, marry only one. This implies obligation when more than one is taken.
The proof is implicit (dalala zanni).
3
Apply the equality principle
All free wives are equal: new or old, virgin or non-virgin, Muslim or Kitabi. Also women with physical or legal conditions have the same right.
This includes the sick, insane, in ihram, etc.
4
Recognize the exception for slave wives
A free wife gets two nights for every one night of a slave wife, based on the inferior right of marriage with a slave.
This applies to mukataba, mudabbara, umm walad as well.
5
Note that obligation is independent of the husband's condition
The husband's sickness does not drop his duty; the wife's sickness does not drop her right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القسم أي في بيان أحكامه البكر كالثيب والجديدة كالقديمة، والمسلمة كالكتابية فيه وللحرّة ضعف الأمة، جاء أي المرأة الجديدة أي الزوجة المسلمة باب القسم: هو التسوية بين الزوجات في البيتوتة والنفقة والسكنى والمأكل والمشرب لا الجماع. (ط)] لما ذكر جواز نكاح أربعة من النسوة للحر وثنتين للعبد لم يكن بد من بيان القسم، وهو بفتح القاف وسكون السين مصدر بمعنى تعيين أنصباء الشركاء لغةً، وبالكسر بمعنى النصيب، ويقال: كلاهما بمعنى النصيب، ولكن بالفتح يستعمل في موضع خاص، بخلاف الكسر ، وشرعاً بالفتح: التسوية بين المنكوحات البيتوتة والنفقة والسكنى لا في المجامعة؛ لأنها تبتني على النشاط، فلا يقدر على التسوية فيها كالمحبة، فلو كان عمل الزوج ليلاً كالحارس قسم نهاراً كما ذكره الشافعية وهو حسن، والقسم واجب؛ لقوله تعالى بعد بيان حل الأربع: ﴿فَإِنْ خِفْتُمْ أَلَّا تَعْدِلُوا فَوَاحِدَةً أَوْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُكُمْ (النساء:۳)، فاستفدنا حل الأربع مقيداً بعدم خوف عدم العدل، وثبوت المنع عن أكثر من واحدة عند خوفه، فعلم إيجابه عند تعددهن. (عيني، فتح) والجديدة كالقديمة مطلقاً سواء كانت الجديدة بكراً أو ثيباً وخصهما مع أن المريضة والرتقاء والقرناء والمراهقة والمجنونة كغيرها؛ لأنهما محل الخلاف والتسوية بينهما في القسم؛ لقوله عليها: "من كانت له امرأتان فمال إلى إحداهما يوم القيامة وشقه مائل" أي مفلوج، رواه أبو داود والنسائي وابن ماجه فهو مطلق لا فصل فيه بين الجديدة والقديمة والبكر والثيب والمسلمة والكتابية؛ ولأن القسم من حقوق النكاح، ولا تفاوت بينهما في ذلك. وقال الشافعي: يقيم عند البكر الجديدة سبعاً وعند الثيب الجديدة ثلاثاً، ولا يحتسب عليها بذلك؛ لما روي عن أنس له أنه قال: سمعت رسول الله له يقول: "للبكر سبع وللثيب ثلاث، ثم يعود إلى أهله" أخرجه الدار قطني، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، قلنا: المراد منه التفضيل بالبداءة للجديدة دون الزيادة بأن يبدأ بالجديدة فيبيت عندها سبعاً إن كانت بكراً أو ثلاثاً إن كانت ثيباً، ثم يبيت عند القديمة كذلك أي سبعاً أو ثلاثاً، أو هو محمول على الصلح؛ ولأن القديمة أولى بالتفضيل؛ لأن الوحشة في جانبها أكثر . (عيني، مستخلص، فتح) فيه: أي في القسم، وكذلك الصحيحة والمريضة والرتقاء والمجنونة التي لا يخاف منها، والحائض والنفساء والحامل والحائل والصغيرة التي يمكن وطيها والمحرمة والمولى منها والمظاهر منها كلها سواء. (ط) ضعف الأمة: [والمدبر والمكاتبة، وعن مالك هما سواء (ع)] أي إن كان له امرأتان إحداهما حرة والأخرى أمة فللحرة ليلتان وللأمة ليلة من القسم؛ لقوله عليا للحرة ليلتان من القسم وللأمة ليلة"، وبذلك قضى الصديق وعلي ما؛ وهو لأن حل الأمة أنقص من حل الحرة بدليل أنه لا يجوز نكاحها معها ولا بعدها، فلا بد من إظهار النقصان في القسم، والمكاتبة والمدبرة وأم الولد بمنزلة الأمة؛ لأن الرق فيهن قائم سواء كن مسلمة أو ذمية والمريضة في القسم كالصحيحة، وكما أن مرضها لا يسقط حقها في القسم، فكذا مرضه لا يكون مسقطاً لحقهن فيه. (مستخلص، فتح)
Chapter of Division: i.e., an explanation of its rulings. The virgin is like the non-virgin, the new [wife] like the old, the Muslim like the Kitabi in it, and for the free [wife] twice the slave [wife]. The chapter on division: it is equality among wives in overnight stay, maintenance, housing, food and drink, not in intercourse. Since he mentioned the permissibility of marrying four free women and two for a slave, it was necessary to explain division. Linguistically, 'al-qasm' (with fatḥa and sukūn) is a verbal noun meaning allocation of partners' shares, and 'al-qism' (with kasra) means the share itself. Technically, al-qasm with fatḥa means equality among married women in overnight stay, maintenance, and housing, not in intercourse because it depends on energy and equality in it is not possible like love. If the husband works at night as a guard, he divides in the day as mentioned by the Shafi'is and it is good. Division is wajib due to His statement after explaining the permissibility of four: 'If you fear you will not be just, then one or what your right hands possess' (4:3). We understand that the permissibility of four is conditioned on not fearing injustice, and prohibition of more than one when fearing it, so we know its obligation when there are multiple wives. The new wife is like the old absolutely, whether the new is virgin or non-virgin, and he specified them even though the sick, closed, having a horn, approaching puberty, and insane are like others because they are the subject of dispute. Equality between them in division is based on his statement: 'Whoever has two wives and inclines to one of them, on the Day of Resurrection his side will be inclined' i.e., paralyzed, narrated by Abu Dawood, al-Nasa'i, and Ibn Majah. It is absolute, no distinction between new and old, virgin and non-virgin, Muslim and Kitabi. Because division is among the rights of marriage, and there is no difference between them in that. Al-Shafi'i said: He stays with a new virgin for seven [nights] and with a new non-virgin for three, and he does not count that against her, based on what Anas narrated: I heard the Messenger of Allah say: 'For a virgin, seven; for a non-virgin, three; then he returns to his family.' Al-Daraqutni narrated it, and Malik and Ahmad agreed. We say: The intended meaning is preference by starting with the new wife without extra days, meaning he begins with the new wife and stays with her for seven if virgin or three if non-virgin, then stays with the old wife likewise, i.e., seven or three; or it is interpreted as a reconciliation. Because the old wife is more deserving of preference since the estrangement on her side is greater. In it: i.e., in division, likewise the healthy and sick, closed, insane who is not feared from, menstruating, postpartum, pregnant, non-pregnant, minor who can be intercourse, in ihram, subject to zihar or li'an, are all equal. Twice the slave: and the mudabbar, mukataba, and according to Malik both are equal. If he has two wives, one free and one slave, the free has two nights and the slave one night in division, due to the statement of Ali: 'For the free, two nights; for the slave, one night.' Abu Bakr and Ali judged accordingly. Because the permissibility of a slave is less than that of a free, evidenced by the fact that it is not permissible to marry her with a free woman or after her, so it is necessary to show deficiency in division. The mukataba, mudabbara, and umm walad are like a slave because slavery is present in them whether they are Muslim or dhimmi. The sick in division is like the healthy, and just as her sickness does not drop her right in division, so too his sickness does not become a ground to drop their rights in it.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on qasm (division of nights) in marriage. It first defines qasm technically: it is equality among wives in overnight stay, maintenance, housing, food, and drink, but not in intercourse, as that depends on desire. The ruling is that qasm is wajib (obligatory), derived from Quran 4:3, where the permissibility of up to four wives is conditional on justice; fearing injustice triggers obligation of one wife only, implying that if one takes more than one, justice becomes wajib. The text then asserts absolute equality among wives regardless of whether they are new or old, virgin or non-virgin, Muslim or Kitabi (Scripture woman), citing a hadith that warns against inclination. A major inter-madhhab dispute arises with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that a husband gives a new virgin wife seven consecutive nights and a new non-virgin three, not counting these against her turn. The Hanafi response is that the hadith cited by Shafi'i (seven for virgin, three for non-virgin) actually refers to starting with the new wife to honor her, not an extra allocation; the old wife is more deserving of extra kindness due to her longer companionship. The text then confirms that all wives are equal in division regardless of physical condition (sick, menstruating, etc.) or legal status (in ihram, etc.). It then addresses the case of a free wife versus a slave wife: the free wife gets two nights for every one night of the slave wife, based on a ruling of Ali and the precedent of Abu Bakr, because the right of marriage with a slave is inherently lesser. This applies also to a mukataba, mudabbara, or umm walad. Finally, sickness of a wife does not diminish her right, nor does the husband's sickness forfeit his obligation.

Key Terms
قسم (qasm): Equality among wives in overnight stay, maintenance, housing, food, and drink, not in intercourse.
بكر (bikr): A virgin wife who has never been married.
ثيب (thayyib): A previously married woman (non-virgin).
أمة (amah): A slave woman (concubine) who is married as a second wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT included in the equality of qasm?
A) Overnight stay
B) Intercourse
C) Maintenance
D) Housing

Key Takeaway
Qasm is a wajib duty for a husband with multiple wives; he must treat all free wives equally in nights, maintenance, and housing, with an exception for slave wives who receive half.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
The New Wife: Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Extra Nights
Presents and resolves the inter-madhhab dispute on new wife's rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that no extra nights are given to a new wife, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the hadith of seven and three.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school holds that a new wife does not get extra nights; the hadith about seven and three refers to a starting preference, not additional days.

A key inter-madhhab dispute in marital law concerns whether a husband must give a new wife extra consecutive nights before resuming the normal rotation. The Hanafi school insists on immediate equality, while the Shafi'i school grants seven nights for a virgin and three for a non-virgin. This lesson examines the proofs on both sides.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi default
All wives are equal in division; a new wife is like the old, regardless of virginity.
The general hadith about not inclining is absolute.
2
Present the Shafi'i position
They give 7 nights for a virgin and 3 for a non-virgin, not counting against her regular turn, based on the hadith narrated by Anas.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis interpret the hadith as a starting preference (giving her priority in the initial nights) not as extra days. Another interpretation: it was a specific reconciliation for a particular situation.
They also argue that the old wife is more deserving of extra kindness due to longer companionship.
4
Note the practical consequence (thamarah)
Hanafi: husband immediately begins normal rotation. Shafi'i: husband stays exclusively with new wife for 7/3 days, then resumes without counting those days as part of her share.
This affects the calendar of nights.
5
Understand the tarjih
The Hanafi position is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, based on the strength of the general command of justice and the interpretation of the specific hadith.
See the hanafi_response in the madhhab_dispute_map.

Source Text

(ط)] لما ذكر جواز نكاح أربعة من النسوة للحر وثنتين للعبد لم يكن بد من بيان القسم، وهو بفتح القاف وسكون السين مصدر بمعنى تعيين أنصباء الشركاء لغةً، وبالكسر بمعنى النصيب، ويقال: كلاهما بمعنى النصيب، ولكن بالفتح يستعمل في موضع خاص، بخلاف الكسر ، وشرعاً بالفتح: التسوية بين المنكوحات البيتوتة والنفقة والسكنى لا في المجامعة؛ لأنها تبتني على النشاط، فلا يقدر على التسوية فيها كالمحبة، فلو كان عمل الزوج ليلاً كالحارس قسم نهاراً كما ذكره الشافعية وهو حسن، والقسم واجب؛ لقوله تعالى بعد بيان حل الأربع: ﴿فَإِنْ خِفْتُمْ أَلَّا تَعْدِلُوا فَوَاحِدَةً أَوْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُكُمْ (النساء:۳)، فاستفدنا حل الأربع مقيداً بعدم خوف عدم العدل، وثبوت المنع عن أكثر من واحدة عند خوفه، فعلم إيجابه عند تعددهن. According to the Hanafi school, the hadith 'For a virgin, seven; for a non-virgin, three' is interpreted to mean:
A) The husband must give extra nights that are not counted.
B) The husband should start his rotation with the new wife, but not give extra days.
C) The husband should give extra nights only if the wife requests.
D) The hadith is abrogated.

Travel with Wives: Choosing a Companion and the Dispute on Drawing Lots
Rules on traveling with a wife and the recommendation of drawing lots.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on drawing lots among wives for travel, state the proof each school uses, and explain the Hanafi tarjih.

Core Idea
A husband may choose any wife for travel; drawing lots is recommended, not obligatory.

This passage addresses a husband's right to choose which wife accompanies him on a journey. While Imam al-Shafi'i argues that lots must be drawn, the Hanafi school holds that the husband may freely choose, though drawing lots is commendable to please hearts. The dispute hinges on whether the Prophet's practice of casting lots was obligatory or voluntary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed element
The act is a husband choosing a wife to travel with. The dispute is whether he must draw lots (qur'a) among his wives.
2
State the Hanafi position
Hanafi: drawing lots is mustahabb (preferable), not wajib. The husband may choose any wife he wishes, even without lots.
3
State the Shafi'i position
Shafi'i: drawing lots is wajib (obligatory). The husband must use lots to select the travel companion.
4
Examine each school's dalil
Shafi'i cites the hadith that the Prophet always drew lots before travel. Hanafi responds with Quran 33:51, which gives the Prophet choice, and argues the Prophet's action was voluntary (tatawwu').
Hanafi also notes that wives have no right to travel, so the Prophet's lots were to please hearts, not to fulfill a duty.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Hanafi: husband may choose arbitrarily; if he draws lots, it's good. Shafi'i: lots must be drawn; choosing arbitrarily without lots is not permissible.

ويُسافر بمن شاء، والقرعة أحب، ولها أن ترجع إن وهبت قسمها للأخرى. الزوج الذي تحت نسوة أي للواحدة منهن على الزوج ويسافر بمن شاء منهن؛ لأنه لا حق لهن في السفر حتى كان للزوج أن لا يستصحب واحدة منهن فكذا له أن يسافر بواحدة أو أكثر بلا إذن من صاحبتها ولا قرعة؛ لأنه قد يتعسر عليه السفر ببعضهن لمرض بها أو سمن أو كثرة أولاد، وقد يأتمن بعضهن في حفظ الأمتعة في السفر أو في البيت، ولكن القرعة أحب تطييباً لقلوبهن، ولم تحتسب عليه أيام سفره حتى لا يقضي لبقية نسائه، وقال الشافعي: تجب القرعة؛ لما روي عن عائشة ما أن النبي كان إذا أراد سفراً أقرع بين نسائه، وأيتهن خرجت قرعتها أو سهمها يخرج بها، متفق عليه، ولنا: أنه لا حق لهن في السفر، وفعله عليها يدل على الاستحباب ونحن نقول به تطييبا لقلوبهن، والدليل عليه أنه عليه لم تكن التسوية واجبة عليه في الحضر، وإنما كان يفعله تفضلاً رمز الحقائق: ۲۲۷/۱] قال تعالى: ﴿تُرْجِي مَنْ تَشَاءُ مِنْهُنَّ وَتُؤْوِي إِلَيْكَ مَنْ تَشَاءُ (الأحزاب: ٥١) فكان ممن يؤوي عائشة وأم سلمة وزينب وحفصة رضي الله عنهن، وممن أرجاه سودة وجويرية قال البيضاوي: ترجي من تشاء أي تؤخرها وتترك مضاجعتها وتؤوي إليك أي تضم إليك وتضاجعها. (فتح) والقرعة: بينهن أحب، فيسافر بمن خرجت قرعتها و لم يحتسب أيام سفره مع التي كانت معه ، لكن يستقبل العدل بينهما. (ط) ولها أن ترجع أي إن رضيت إحدى الزوجات أن تترك قسمها لصاحبتها جاز ولها الرجوع على الزوج، والدليل على جواز هبة القسم من ضرتها أنه لما كبرت سودة لما أراد النبي فسألته أن لا يفعل وجعلت يومها لعائشة هما الأن تحشر يوم القيامة مع أزواجه، فأمسكها النبي وإنما كان لها الرجوع بعد الهبة؛ لأن الإسقاط إنما يكون في القائم؛ لأن ما ليس كذلك كان الرجوع عنه امتناعا لا إسقاطاً، فكان بمنزلة العارية وللمعير أن يرجع متى شاء. (مستخلص، فتح) للأخرى لأنها أسقطت حقا لم يجب بعد فلا يسقط كالمعير يرجع في العارية متى شاء. (ع)
And he travels with whomever he wishes, and drawing lots is preferable. And she may retract if she has gifted her division to another. The husband who has wives: i.e., for one of them upon the husband. He travels with whomever he wishes of them, because women have no right in travel; even the husband may not take any of them, so similarly he may travel with one or more without permission from the other and without drawing lots, because it may be difficult for him to travel with some due to her sickness, obesity, or many children, and he may trust some to guard luggage during travel or at home. But drawing lots is preferable to please their hearts. And the days of his travel are not counted against him such that he would have to make up for the remaining wives. Al-Shafi'i said: Drawing lots is obligatory, based on the narration from Aisha that the Prophet when intending travel would draw lots among his wives, and whichever's lot came out, he would take her, agreed upon. Our evidence: women have no right in travel, and his action indicates recommendation, and we adhere to it to please their hearts. The evidence is that he was not obligated to equalize in residence; he only did it as a favor. Allah said: 'You defer whom you wish of them and bring near to you whom you wish' (33:51). He would bring near Aisha, Umm Salama, Zainab, Hafsa, and defer Sawda, Juwayriya. Al-Baydawi said: 'Turji' means you delay and leave her bed; 'tu'wi' means you bring near and share the bed. And drawing lots among them is preferable, so he travels with the one whose lot comes out, and the days of travel with her are not counted, but then he starts the equal rotation anew. She may retract: If one of the wives is pleased to leave her division for her co-wife, it is permissible, and she has the right to retract from the husband. The evidence for the permissibility of gifting division to the co-wife is that when Sawda grew old and the Prophet wanted to divorce her, she asked him not to and gave her day to Aisha, saying: 'I hope to be resurrected with the wives of the Prophet,' so he kept her. She had the right to retract after the gift because the dropping (isqat) is only applicable to an existing right; since it was not, retracting is refraining, not dropping, so it is like a loan (ariyah) and the lender may retract whenever he wishes.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage discusses two separate issues: (1) traveling with wives and (2) a wife waiving her turn. First, the husband has the right to choose any wife to accompany him on travel because wives have no claim to be taken on a journey. He may even travel alone. However, it is recommended to draw lots among them to avoid jealousy and please their hearts. The days spent on travel with one wife do not require makeup days for the others. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that drawing lots is obligatory, citing the Prophet's practice. The Hanafi response is that the Prophet's action was voluntary, not obligatory, as indicated by Quran 33:51 where Allah gave him the choice to defer any wife. Therefore, the husband's choice is valid, but drawing lots is better. Second, if a wife voluntarily gives up her turn (qasm) in favor of another wife, it is valid. However, she retains the right to retract that gift at any time, because the right is not yet established as a fixed obligation; her waiver is like lending a right (ariyah), and the lender can reclaim it. This is based on the incident of Sawda gifting her day to Aisha; the Prophet accepted it, and she could later revoke. The key principle is that a future right can be waived but not irrevocably; a wife cannot drop an obligation that is not yet due.

Key Terms
قرعة (qur'a): Drawing lots to select among wives for travel, used to avoid favoritism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on drawing lots (qur'a) when a husband chooses a wife for travel?
A) It is wajib
B) It is mustahabb
C) It is haram
D) It is makruh

Key Takeaway
A husband may freely choose which wife to travel with; drawing lots is recommended but not obligatory.

A Wife's Waiver of Her Turn: Validity and Revocability
Rules on a wife gifting her turn to another and the revocability of such a gift.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a wife's waiver of her turn (hibat al-qasm), explain its revocability using the analogy of ariyah, and apply it to a scenario.

Core Idea
A wife may gift her turn to a co-wife, but she retains the right to revoke it at any time.

This passage addresses a situation where one wife voluntarily gives up her share of the husband's time (qasm) to another wife. The Hanafi school rules that such a waiver is valid but revocable, likened to a loan (ariyah). The incident of Sawda gifting her day to Aisha serves as the primary evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue: a wife voluntarily gives up her right to her husband's overnight stay (qasm) in favor of another wife.
This is called hibat al-qasm (gifting the division).
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The waiver is valid (ja'iz), but the wife may retract it at any time.
3
Identify the evidence
The evidence is the incident of Sawda bint Zam'a, who, when she grew old, gifted her day to Aisha to avoid divorce. The Prophet accepted this arrangement.
This shows permissibility.
4
Understand the legal reasoning
The waiver is not an eternal forfeiture because the right is not yet existing (it is a future right). Dropping (isqat) applies only to existing rights. Therefore, the waiver is like a loan (ariyah): the lender can demand it back.
The analogy to ariyah is a qiyas.
5
Note the practical implication
If a wife gifts her turn and later changes her mind, she may revoke the gift and reclaim her turn. The husband must honour that.

هبة القسم (hibat al-qasm): A wife voluntarily giving up her night(s) to another wife.

The Quantity of Fosterage Prohibition: Any Amount Suffices
Defines rada' and argues Hanafi position that any quantity suffices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the minimum quantity of fosterage and explain why the Quranic absolute wording takes precedence over the abrogated hadith.

Core Idea
Any drop of breast milk that reaches the stomach creates fosterage prohibition — no minimum number.

Fosterage (radāʿ) is a cause of marriage prohibition just like lineage. But how much breastfeeding is enough? The Hanafi school holds that any amount, little or much, suffices, while Shāfiʿīs require five full feedings. This lesson examines the textual proofs and the reasoning behind the Hanafi view, which is based on the absolute wording of the Quran and the general practice of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Define fosterage (radāʿ) according to the Hanafi school
Identify the technical definition: the infant's sucking from a human breast within the specific period of infancy, with milk reaching the stomach.
The definition excludes milk from animals or reaching the stomach through other means like sniffing.
2
Identify the dispute on quantity
The Hanafi position: any amount prohibits. The Shāfiʿī position: only five full sucklings. Note the other schools.
3
Analyze the proofs for each position
For the Hanafis: Quran 4:23 (absolute without number) and general hadiths. For the Shāfiʿīs: the hadith of ʿĀʾishah about abrogation and the hadith 'one or two sucklings do not prohibit'.
The Hanafi response argues the Shāfiʿī hadiths are abrogated or weak.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Hanafi, even a single feeding creates full fosterage relations. Under Shāfiʿī, only after five complete feedings.
This affects who one can marry in cases of partial breastfeeding.
5
Conclude with the tarjih
The Hanafi position is held by the majority of scholars and is the muftā bihī view.
The commentator cites al-Nawawī and al-Layth to support this.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الرضاع
أحكام الرضاع
كتاب الرضاع
أي في بيان أحكامه
هو مص الرّضيع من ثدي الآدمية في وقت مخصوص، وحرم به وإن
أي الرضاع شرعا قليلاً أو كثيراً
وهو مدة الرضاع
بالرضاع
ك ف
كتاب الرضاع [جعل في الديوان فتح الراء أصلا، والكسر لغة والفعل من باب علم أصل، ومن باب ضرب لغة أهل نجد. (مسكين)] لما كان المقصود من النكاح الولد، وهو لا يعيش في ابتداء أمره غالباً إلا بالرضاع، وكان له أحكام تتعلق به وهي من آثار النكاح المتأخرة عنه جعله آخر أحكامه ومقتضى هذا التعليل أن تعنون مسائل الرضاع بالباب كما جعله بعض الشارحين، والمناسبة بينهما: أن الرضاع سبب للحرمة كما أن النكاح سبب لحرمة المصاهرة، فهما نظيران من حيث أن النكاح سبب للحل والرضاع سبب للحرمة، وهو بكسر الراء وفتحها وكذلك الرضاعة وأنكر الأصمعي الكسر مع الهاء، ويقال: لئيم راضع الذي يرضع إبله أو غنمه ولا يحلبها كي لا يستمع صوت حلبه فيطلب منه. (عيني، فتح) هو مص الرضيع هذا التعريف منقوض طرداً وعكساً، أما الأول: فلأنه قد يوجد المص ولا رضاعة إن لم يصل إلى الجوف، وأما الثاني فلأن المص قد ينتفي ويثبت الرضاع كما لو وصل إلى جوفه يصب في وسط الفم أو بالسعوط من الأنف والجواب: أنه أراد الوصول إلى الجوف من المنفذين وقوله : من ثدي الآدمية" ولو بكراً ميتة أو آئسة، وخرج به الرجل والشاة. (فتح)
وحرم به وفيه خلاف في موضعين: الأول : أن الرضاع قليله وكثيره سواء عندنا، وقال الشافعي: لا يحرم إلا بخمس رضعات مشبعات و به قال أحمد وعنه ثلاث رضعات؛ لما روي عن عائشة ما أنها قالت: كان فيما نزل من القرآن عشر رضعات معلومات يحرمن ثم نسخن بخمس معلومات، فتوفي رسول الله وهي فيما يقرأ من القرآن، رواه مسلم وعنها أنها قالت لا تحرم المصة "والمصتان وفي لفظ: "لا تحرم الإملاجة والإملاجتان"، رواه مسلم، فالحديث الثاني قضى لنفي مذهبنا، والأول لإثبات مذهبه. ولنا قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأُمَّهَاتُكُمُ اللَّاتِي أَرْضَعْنَكُمْ وَأَخَوَاتُكُمْ مِنَ الرَّضَاعَةِ ﴾ (النساء:۲۳) علقه بفعل الإرضاع من غير قيد بالعدد، والتقييد به زيادة، وهو نسخ، وإطلاق الأحاديث منها ما رواه البخاري ومسلم أنه علي قال: "يحرم من الرضاع ما يحرم من الرحم" وفي لفظ "ما يحرم من النسب" ومنها: حديث عائشة أنه عليها قال: "إن الله حرم من الرضاع ما حرم من الولادة" متفق عليه، وما رواه منسوخ، روي عن ابن عباس لما أنه قال: قوله : "لا تحرم الرضعة والرضعتان" كان، فأما اليوم فالرضعة الواحدة تحرم، فجعله منسوخاً، حكاه عنه أبو بكر الرازي، ومثله عن ابن مسعود ، وقال ابن بطال: أحاديث عائشة الالها المضطربة ؛ لأنه يرويه ابن زيد مرة عن النبي علية، ومرة عن عائشة، ومرة عن أبيه، فوجب تركها، والرجوع إلى كتاب الله، ومذهبنا مذهب علي وابن عباس وابن عمر وابن مسعود له وجم جمهور التابعين -
وقال النووي: هو قول جمهور العلماء، وقال الليث : أجمع المسلمون على أن كثير الرضاع وقليله يحرم في المهد كما يفطر الصائم،
Book of Fosterage. Rulings of Fosterage. Book of Fosterage: i.e., explaining its rulings. It is the suckling of the infant from the breast of a human female in a specific time, and prohibition is established by it even if little or much. And it is the period of fosterage. Commentary: The definition is criticized both in inclusion and exclusion... The first dispute: that fosterage, little and much, is equal according to us. Al-Shafi'i said: it does not prohibit except by five full sucklings... Our proof is His statement: 'And your mothers who nursed you and your sisters from fosterage' (4:23), attached to the act of nursing without restriction by number... Al-Nawawi said: it is the saying of the majority of scholars. Al-Layth said: the Muslims have agreed that much and little fosterage in infancy prohibits just as it breaks the fast of a fasting person.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the quantity of breastfeeding that establishes marriage prohibition. The Hanafi school holds that any amount, little or much, creates the prohibition, based on the absolute wording of Quran 4:23 and general hadiths. The Shafi'i school requires five full sucklings, citing hadiths from Aisha about abrogation. The Hanafi response argues that the Shafi'i proofs are abrogated or weak, and the majority of scholars (including many Companions) agree with the Hanafi position. The passage also defines rada' as the infant's sucking from a human breast, with conditions for the milk reaching the stomach. This is from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Key Terms
الرضاع (al-rida' / al-rada'): Suckling of an infant from the breast of a human female within the specific period (duration of fosterage).
الرضعة (al-rad'ah): A single act of sucking; in the context of prohibition, the minimum quantity that reaches the stomach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Exceptions to Fosterage Prohibition: The Permissible Marriages
Ruling on exceptions to fosterage prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two specific relatives who are permissible to marry through fosterage but prohibited through lineage, and state the Hanafi legal reasoning for the exception.

Core Idea
Fosterage prohibits what lineage prohibits, except the mother of your foster-brother and the sister of your foster-son.

In Hanafi fiqh, fosterage (rada') creates the same marriage prohibitions as blood lineage (nasab), with a few specific exceptions. This lesson examines a key passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq that names two such exceptions: the mother of one's foster-brother and the sister of one's foster-son. Understanding these exceptions helps clarify the boundaries of fosterage prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The matn states: 'Within thirty months, what is prohibited by lineage is prohibited by fosterage.' This establishes the default rule that any marriage prohibited by lineage is also prohibited by fosterage.
2
Identify the exception clause
The matn continues: 'except the mother of his brother and the sister of his son.' These are the two exceptions where the prohibition does not apply.
3
Understand why these are prohibited in lineage
In lineage, the mother of one's brother is either one's own mother (if full or maternal brother) or a wife of one's father (if paternal brother) — both are prohibited. The sister of one's son is either one's daughter or stepdaughter — also prohibited.
4
Understand why fosterage does not create the same prohibition
Fosterage creates a relationship through nursing, but it does not create the direct maternal or paternal ties that would make these women mahram. Therefore, they remain permissible to marry.
5
Note additional exceptions from the commentary
The commentary adds further exceptions: mother of one's foster-grandchildren, grandmother of one's foster-child, etc., all permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
