A) He must wait until she recovers.
B) He may make verbal fay' by saying 'I have returned'.
C) He must divorce her.
D) The Ila' is automatically invalidated.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school fixes the Ila' period for a slave woman at two months based on analogy with 'idda, and permits verbal fay' when intercourse is impossible.

Fay' (Return) in Ila': Types, Conditions, and the Shafi'i Dispute
Explains definition, types, conditions, and dispute on fay' in Ila'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between original and substitute fay', state the conditions for valid verbal fay', and evaluate the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
When actual intercourse is impossible, verbal fay' suffices, annulling the Ila' regarding divorce but preserving the oath.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies that fay' can be either original (intercourse) or substitute (verbal declaration) when intercourse is physically impossible. It also presents a key dispute with al-Shafi'i, who rejects verbal fay' entirely. The Hanafi position is grounded in the practice of the Companions and the underlying purpose of Ila'.

Guided Path
1
Define fay' linguistically and legally
Understand the linguistic root of fay' (return) and its legal application as the act of ending Ila' by returning to the wife, analogous to raj'a in divorce.
The verb 'fā'a' is used in the Qur'an (2:226) to mean 'they return'.
2
Identify the two types of fay'
Distinguish between the original (aṣl) which is intercourse, and the substitute (khalaf) which is a verbal declaration like 'I have returned to you'. Recognize that the substitute is only valid when the husband is actually unable to have intercourse.
Legal inability (e.g., iḥrām) does not count; only genuine physical impossibility.
3
Analyze the effect of each type on Ila' and the oath
Verbal fay' cancels the Ila' regarding divorce only, so no divorce occurs at the end of the period. However, the oath remains; if he later has intercourse, he breaks the oath and must pay kaffara. Intercourse as fay' cancels both the Ila' and the oath.
This partial effect is unique to the substitute.
4
Examine the Shafi'i objection and its rebuttal
Al-Shafi'i argues that only intercourse can be fay' because it is the right violated. The Hanafi rebuttal relies on the statements of 'Ali and Ibn Mas'ud that a sick person may make fay' with his tongue, and on the purpose of preventing harm, which is achieved by verbal return when intercourse is impossible.
The text says 'the correct is what we have said' (aṣaḥḥ mā qulnā), indicating the Hanafi view is relied upon.
5
Apply the two conditions for valid verbal fay'
According to the Hanafi school, verbal fay' is conditional on: (1) the declaration must be made during the Ila' period, and (2) the inability must persist throughout the period (the text cuts off, but it implies the inability continues until the end).
These conditions ensure that the return is genuine and not a ruse.

Source Text

اعلم أن الفيء عبارة عن الرجوع يقال: "فاء الظل" إذا رجع، ولما قصد المولى باليمين منع حقها من الوطء سمي الرجوع عنه فيئا، قال تعالى: فَإِنْ فَاءُ والله (البقرة: ٢٢٦) أي رجعوا عن قصدهم، فالفيء في الإيلاء بمنزلة الرجعة في الطلاق. ثم الفيء على نوعين أصل وخلف، والأصل هو الجماع والخلف أن يقول بلسانه فئت إليها، وإنما يصح الفيء باللسان إذا عجز عن الأصل بسبب مرضها أو مرضه ونحوهما والمراد العجز حقيقة، فإن عجز حكماً لا حكم الإيلاء بأن كان محرما لا يكون فيؤه إلا بالوطء؛ لأنه لما كان قادراً حقيقة على الوطء لا يتحول الحكم إلى ما هو خلف عن الوطء. ثم إن قال ذلك أو ما دل عليه كراجعتك وارتجعتك سقط الإيلاء عندنا حتى لا يقع الطلاق بمضي المدة، لكن إذا وطئها بعد المدة أو قبلها بعد زوال الأعذار المذكورة فعليه الكفارة لتحقق الحنث به؛ لأن يمينه باقية في حق إن بطلت في حق الطلاق فالفيء باللسان يبطل الإيلاء في حق الطلاق فقط، والفيء بالوطء يبطله في حق الطلاق والحنث جميعاً، وقال الشافعي له: لا فيء إلا بالجماع، ولا يصح الفيء باللسان أصلا؛ لأنه ظلمها بمنع حقها وهو الوطء، فيكون إيفاؤه به؛ ولأنه لو كان فيئا لكان حنثا؛ لأن المعلق بالفيء حکمان وجوب الكفارة، وامتناع حكم الفرقة، والفيء باللسان لا يعتبر في حق أحد الحكمين، فكذا في حق حكم الآخر، وإليه ذهب الطحاوي، وعن الشافعي حه يقول: ندمت على ما فعلت. أحمد يقول: متى قدرت جامعتك. والأصح ما قلنا من أن الفيء باللسان عند العجز خلف عن الوطء لقول علي الله وابن مسعود ه: "يفيء المريض باللسان"، وكفى بهما قدوة؛ ولأن وقوع الطلاق عند مضي المدة باعتبار التعنت والإضرار بها، وذلك ينعدم بالفيء باللسان عند العجز عن الفيء بالجماع؛ لأن الفيء عبارة عن الرجوع، وذلك لا يوجد بهما إلا أن الفيء باللسان مشروط بشرطين عندنا أحدهما أن يكون قوله: فئت أو ما يدل عليه في مدة الإيلاء، والثاني: استمرار العجز من وعند
Know that fay' is a term for return; it is said: 'the shadow fay'a' when it returns. When the Mul intended by his oath to prevent her right to intercourse, the return from it is called fay'. Allah said: 'Then if they return' (al-Baqara 2:226), i.e., they returned from their intention. Therefore fay' in Ila' is like raj'a in divorce. Then fay' is of two types: original and substitute. The original is intercourse, and the substitute is that he says with his tongue: 'I have returned to her.' Fay' by tongue is only valid when he is unable to perform the original due to her illness, his illness, or similar. What is meant is actual inability. If there is a legal inability (not actual), such as being in ihram, his fay' is only by intercourse; because since he is actually capable of intercourse, the ruling does not transfer to a substitute for intercourse. Then if he says that or something indicating it like 'I have taken you back' or 'I have returned you', the Ila' drops according to us, so that divorce does not occur by passage of the period. But if he has intercourse after the period or before it after the removal of the mentioned excuses, then the expiation is obligatory due to the occurrence of breaking the oath; because his oath remains in effect regarding that, even if it is void regarding divorce. So fay' by tongue cancels Ila' regarding divorce only, and fay' by intercourse cancels it regarding both divorce and the oath-breaking. Al-Shafi'i said: There is no fay' except by intercourse, and fay' by tongue is not valid at all; because he wronged her by preventing her right, which is intercourse, so the fulfilment of it is by that. And because if it were fay', it would be a violation (breaking of oath), because the two rulings attached to fay' are obligation of expiation and prevention of separation; fay' by tongue is not considered in one of these rulings, so likewise regarding the other ruling. And al-Tahawi inclined to that. And it is reported from al-Shafi'i that he said: 'I regret what I did.' Ahmad says: 'Whenever you are able, I will have intercourse with you.' The correct is what we have said, that fay' by tongue when unable is a substitute for intercourse, due to the saying of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and Ibn Mas'ud: 'The ill patient makes fay' by his tongue.' And they suffice as exemplars. And because the occurrence of divorce at the end of the period is based on stubbornness and harm, and that is nonexistent with fay' by tongue when unable for intercourse, because fay' is a term for return, and that is not achieved by them except that fay' by tongue is conditional on two conditions according to us: one is that he says 'I have returned' or what indicates it during the Ila' period, and the second is the continuation of inability from and when ...
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage delves into the concept of fay' (return) in Ila'. It begins by defining fay' linguistically as 'return', citing the Qur'anic verse. It then explains that fay' is analogous to raj'a (taking back) in divorce. There are two types: original (intercourse) and substitute (verbal declaration). Verbal fay' is only valid when the husband is genuinely unable to have intercourse due to illness, the wife's illness, or similar physical obstacles. Legal impediments like ihram do not count; the husband must actually be unable. If verbal fay' is done during the Ila' period, the Ila' is annulled regarding divorce, so that divorce does not fall after four months. However, the oath remains intact; if the husband later has intercourse (after the period or after the excuse ends), he must pay the expiation (kaffara) for breaking his oath. If he does fay' by intercourse, both the Ila' and the oath are nullified. The text then presents the Shafi'i view: only intercourse counts as fay'; verbal fay' is invalid, because the right violated is intercourse, so only intercourse can rectify it, and if verbal fay' were valid, it would itself be a violation (since it does not fulfill the conditions). The Hanafi response is based on the practice of 'Ali and Ibn Mas'ud, who affirmed that a sick person can make fay' verbally. Additionally, the purpose of the period is to prevent harm to the wife; if the husband expresses intent to return verbally when unable to cohabit, that harm is removed. Finally, two conditions for verbal fay' are mentioned: it must be said within the Ila' period, and the inability must persist throughout the period (the text cuts off).

Key Terms
فيء (fay’): Return to the wife by ending the vow of abstention; can be through intercourse or verbally when unable.
أصل وخلف (aṣl wa khalaf): Original and substitute; the original fay' is intercourse, the substitute is verbal declaration.
حنث (ḥinth): Breaking of oath; requires expiation (kaffara).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The wife must agree to the return.

Key Takeaway
Verbal fay' is a valid substitute for intercourse in Ila' when the husband is actually unable, preventing divorce but preserving the oath, based on Sahabi practice and the purpose of preventing harm.

Nikah — Marriage

Definition and Scope of Khul' Rights
Defines khul' and its effect on marriage rights.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of khul', identify which rights it automatically drops, and apply the Hanafi ruling on release from non-marriage debts.

Core Idea
Khul' drops only rights arising from the marriage contract, not other debts.

Khul' (divorce initiated by the wife with compensation) and mubara'ah (mutual release) are mechanisms to end a marriage while settling financial claims. This passage defines their core effect: they automatically cancel all rights and obligations that are directly connected to the marriage, such as the unpaid dower (mahr). However, rights from other sources, like a personal loan, are not automatically waived. Understanding this scope is crucial for correctly advising couples seeking separation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is khul'? How does it legally terminate the marriage? The passage states that khul' drops 'every right that each spouse has over the other relating to the marriage.' This is the key legal effect: automatic release from marriage-related claims.
The ruling is that khul' is a valid (sahih) way to end marriage, producing a ba'in (irrevocable) talaq.
2
Define the scope of rights dropped
Which rights are 'relating to the marriage'? The commentary specifies that this includes mahr, maintenance during iddah (if stipulated), and other obligations directly arising from the marriage contract. Rights from other causes, like a debt from a separate loan, are not automatically dropped.
The restriction 'muta'alliq bi al-nikah' is crucial.
3
Examine exceptions and conditions
Can the parties agree to drop additional rights? The text discusses stipulations regarding iddah maintenance, child support, and housing. It also clarifies that release from housing (sukna) cannot be waived because the wife's leaving would be disobedience. However, she can waive the monetary cost of housing.
Stipulations are valid if they are purely her right and do not contradict the shari'a.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on other debts
Does khul' release from debts not connected to marriage? According to Zahir al-Riwayah (the core transmissions of Imam Muhammad), no. But a rare narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa says yes. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that only marriage-related rights are dropped.
This is a thamarah al-khilaf: it matters if the wife owes the husband a separate loan.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Take a case where a wife initiates khul' and owes her husband $1000 from a loan. According to the mufta bihi view, the loan remains due. However, if they explicitly include the loan in the khul' agreement, it is valid.
Always check the contract wording and the parties' intentions.

من الزوجين
الثابت عند الخلع
معلوم كان للزوج ما سمت ،له و لم يبق لأحدهما قبل صاحبه دعوى في المهر

وقيد بقوله: "مما يتعلق بالنكاح"؛ لأن غيره من الحقوق لا دخل له فيها؛ لأن وجوبه ليس بسبب النكاح، ونفقة العدة لم تجب بعد، ولكن لو شرطا البراءة منها سقطت ولو شرطا البراءة من نفقة الولد الصغير وهي مؤونة ينظر، فإن وقتا له وقتاً كالسنة ونحوه صح، وإلا فلا، ولا يصح إبراؤها عن السكنى؛ لأن خروجها معصية، ولو أبرأته عن مؤونة السكنى بأن التزمتها أو سكنت ملكها صح مشروطاً في الخلع؛ لأنه خالص حقها. ثم هل تقع البراءة من دين آخر سوى دين النكاح ؟ في ظاهر الرواية لا، وفي رواية الحسن عن الإمام تقع، وكذلك المبارأة هل توجب البراءة عن سائر الديون؟ فيه اختلاف المشايخ والصحيح أنها لا توجب. أما إذا كان العقد بلفظ الطلاق على مال فهل تقع البراءة عن الحقوق المتعلقة بالنكاح؟ في ظاهر الرواية لا تقع؛ لأن لفظ الطلاق لا يدل على إسقاط الحق الواجب بالنكاح، وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة تقع البراءة عنها؛ لإتمام المقصود، ولو كان الخلع بلفظ البيع والشراء فالصحيح أنه كالخلع والمبارأة، وعندهما الجواب فيه كالجواب في الخلع، ولو قال لامرأته: "خالعتك" فقبلت المرأة، يقع الطلاق وتقع البراءة إن كان عليه ،مهر وإن لم يكن يجب عليها رد ما ساق إليها من المهر؛ لأن المال مذكور عرفاً بذكر الخلع، ولا يسقط دين واجب عليه بسبب آخر، ومهر لها عليه في نكاح ثابت، والسقوط فيما إذا كان الخلع الصادر بين الزوجين بصيغة المفاعلة. (عيني، فتح) كل حق بالنصب مفعول "يسقط" أي كل حق ثابت في النكاح الصحيح. (ط، ع) حتى لو خالعها : [بيان لإسقاط الخلع والمبارأة كل حق لكل منهما على صاحبه مما يتعلق بالنكاح أي حتى لو خالع رجل امرأته (ع)] لا فرق بين "خلعتك" و "خالعتك" إذا ذكر العوض، أما إذا لم يذكر، بينهما فرق من وجهين: الأول: أن "خلعتك" لا يتوقف على القبول، بخلاف "خالعتك"، الثاني: لا براءة في الأول بخلاف الثاني. (فتح) بارأها بأن قال بارأتك على هذا الثوب أو قالت بارئني على هذا الثوب. (ع)

بمال معلوم [سواء كان عيناً أو ديناً، وسواء كان ثوباً أو عبداً أو درهماً قيد به؛ لأنه لو كان مجهولاً جهالة غير مستدركة كما لو قالت على ما في هذا البيت وليس فيه شيء، أو على ما يثمر نخلي العام بطلت التسمية وردت ما قبضته من المهر، بخلاف ما لو كانت غير فاحشة كثوب هروي. (فتح) دعوى في المهر: حتى لا يجب عليها رد ما قبضت ولا عليه دفع ما لم تقبض. (ع) وهو اسم للادعاء وألفها للتأنيث فلا ،تنون وجمعها دعاوى بالفتح كفتوى وفتاوى كذا في المغرب. (مسكين)
Khul': Every right that each spouse has over the other relating to the marriage (even if he divorces her by khul' or mubara'ah for a known compensation, the husband gets what she specified, and no claim regarding mahr remains between them). Commentary: 'Maternal breastfeeding'? No: 'the woman to the husband', 'the two spouses' meaning 'with each other'. It is restricted by 'relating to the marriage' because other rights are not involved, as their obligation is not due to marriage, and iddah maintenance is not yet obligatory, but if they stipulate release from it, it drops. If they stipulate release from maintenance of a small child, which is a provision, one looks: if they specify a time like a year or similar, it is valid; otherwise not. Release from housing is not valid because her leaving is disobedience. If she releases him from the cost of housing by undertaking it or housing herself in her own property, it is valid as a condition in khul' because it is purely her right. Then: Does release from debts other than the marriage debt occur? In Zahir al-Riwayah, no; in the transmission of al-Hasan from al-Imam, yes. Likewise, does mubara'ah necessitate release from all debts? There is ikhtilaf among the mashayikh; the correct view is that it does not. If the contract is with the word talaq for compensation, does release from rights related to marriage occur? In Zahir al-Riwayah, it does not occur because the word talaq does not indicate dropping a right due to marriage. In the transmission of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, release occurs due to completing the purpose. If khul' is with the wording of sale and purchase, the correct view is that it is like khul' and mubara'ah; according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the answer in it is like the answer in khul'. If a man says to his wife: 'I divorce you by khul'' and she accepts, talaq occurs and release occurs if there is mahr upon him, and she does not have to return what he gave her of mahr because the compensation is mentioned customarily by mentioning khul', and a wajib debt due to another cause is not dropped, and she has mahr upon him in a valid marriage. The dropping is when the khul' issued between the spouses is in the form of mufa'alah. ('Ayni, Fath) 'Every right' is in the accusative as the object of 'yasqutu' (falls), i.e., every right established in a valid marriage. (T, 'A) 'Even if he divorces her by khul'': explanation that khul' and mubara'ah drop every right each has over the other relating to marriage, i.e., even if a man divorces his wife by khul'. ('A) There is no difference between 'khala'tuka' and 'khala'tuka' when the compensation is mentioned; if not mentioned, there is a difference in two ways: first, 'khala'tuka' does not require acceptance, unlike 'khala'tuka'; second, there is no release in the first unlike the second. (Fath) 'Mubara'ah' is when he says 'I declare you free from claims on this garment' or she says 'Declare me free on this garment.' ('A) 'Known compensation' whether it is a specific chattel or a debt, whether a garment, a slave, or dirhams. It is restricted because if it is unknown with non-remediable ignorance, as if she says 'on what is in this house' and there is nothing in it, or 'on what my palm trees yield this year', the naming is invalid and she returns whatever she received of the mahr, unlike if the ignorance is not excessive like a Herawi garment. (Fath) 'Claim in the mahr' means that she does not have to return what she received and he does not have to pay what she did not receive. ('A) It is the noun for claim, its alif is for feminine, so it is not nunated; its plural is dawa' (with fath) like fatwa and fatawa; thus in al-Mughrib. (Miskin)
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on the nature of khul' (divorce initiated by the wife with compensation) and mubara'ah (mutual release). The author begins by stating that khul' drops every right that each spouse has over the other that is connected to the marriage contract. This means that once a valid khul' occurs, neither party can claim anything from the other related to the marriage, such as the mahr (dower) that was unpaid or already given. The commentary expands on this by clarifying the scope: rights not connected to marriage, such as debts from other transactions, are not automatically dropped unless specifically stipulated. There is a discussion on whether release from iddah maintenance can be stipulated – it can, even though it is not yet obligatory. Release from maintenance of a small child is conditional upon specifying a time. Release from housing (sukna) is not valid because the wife's leaving after divorce (during iddah) would be disobedience, so she cannot waive that right. However, if she releases him from the monetary cost of housing by undertaking it herself or providing her own property, it is valid because it is purely her right. The commentary then addresses whether khul' also releases from other debts besides the marriage debt. In Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books of Imam Muhammad), the position is that it does not release from other debts. However, in a rare transmission from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa, it does release. Similarly, for mubara'ah, the correct opinion (sahih) according to later scholars is that it does not release from other debts. When the contract uses the word 'talaq' (i.e., 'I divorce you on condition of compensation'), in Zahir al-Riwayah, release from marriage rights does not occur because the word talaq itself does not imply waiver. But in al-Hasan's transmission, release does occur because the purpose of the transaction is to end all ties. If khul' uses the wording of sale and purchase (e.g., 'I sell you your divorce'), the correct view is that it is like khul' and mubara'ah in effect. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the answer is the same as for khul'. If a man says 'khal'atuka' (I divorce you by khul') and she accepts, talaq occurs and release from marriage rights occurs, and she does not have to return any mahr she already received because the mention of compensation is customary with the word khul'. The passage also distinguishes between 'khala'tuka' (without acceptance needed) and 'khala'tuka' (requires acceptance) when compensation is not mentioned. The phrase 'mufa'alah' (mutual action) indicates that khul' in its proper sense requires mutual exchange.

Key Terms
الخلع (al-khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband; it drops all marriage-related rights between them.
المبارأة (al-mubara'ah): Mutual release from all claims, including marriage rights; similar to khul' but with mutual consent without specified compensation.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted rulings from the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (the 'apparent' or well-known transmissions).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Zahir al-Riwayah), which of the following is automatically dropped when a valid khul' occurs?
A) A personal loan the wife gave to her husband before marriage
B) The husband's obligation to pay the unpaid dower (mahr)
C) The wife's obligation to return gifts received during marriage
D) The husband's right to custody of children

Key Takeaway
Khul' drops only rights arising from the marriage contract; other debts remain unless explicitly included in the agreement.

Sixteen Cases of Mahr in Khul': A Parsing Exercise
Enumeration of sixteen khul' cases with mahr effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the sixteen scenarios of khul' and state the correct ruling on mahr status, distinguishing between qiyas and istihsan where applicable.

Core Idea
In khul', mahr status depends on timing, naming of compensation, and receipt – and Hanafi istihsan often overrides strict qiyas.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq enumerates sixteen possible cases in a khul' transaction, covering all combinations of whether compensation is named (none, full mahr, part mahr, other property), timing (before or after consummation), and whether the mahr has been received. The Hanafi school applies both qiyas and istihsan, often preferring istihsan to avoid hardship. Understanding these cases gives precision to a common area of family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three variables
Recognize that the enumeration is based on: (1) what compensation is named (none, full mahr, part mahr, other property), (2) timing relative to consummation, and (3) whether the mahr has been received (paid) or not. These produce 4 x 2 x 2 = 16 cases.
The text explicitly states: إما أن لا يسميا شيئاً أو سميا المهر أو بعضه أو مالاً آخر (either they name nothing, or they name the mahr or part of it, or other property).
2
Understand the base ruling for unnamed compensation
Cases 1-4: when no specific compensation is named, each spouse is freed from the other's marriage-related rights. The mahr, if unpaid, is dropped; if paid, it remains with the wife. This is the effect of mutual release (khul' without stipulated compensation).
The text says: برئ كل منهما عن حق الآخر مما لزمه في النكاح (each is free from the other's right regarding what was obligatory in the marriage).
3
Analyze cases where full mahr is named (cases 5-8)
When the full mahr is named as compensation, after consummation (case 5,6) the husband gets the entire named amount if paid. Before consummation (cases 7-8), a qiyas/istihsan split emerges: by qiyas, he gets the named mahr plus half the mahr as in talaq before consummation; by istihsan, he only gets the named amount (if paid) or nothing extra (if unpaid).
Case 7: يرجع عليها بألف وخمسمائة ... قياساً، وبالألف المقبوض فقط استحساناً.
4
Analyze cases where part mahr is named (cases 9-12)
When part of the mahr is named, e.g., one-tenth, after consummation (cases 9-10) the husband gets that fraction (one-tenth) if mahr paid; if unpaid, the fraction is due and the rest of the mahr drops. Before consummation (cases 11-12) similar qiyas/istihsan appears: by qiyas he gets the fraction plus half the mahr; by istihsan he gets only the fraction (half of the fraction? Actually case 11: يرجع بخمسين درهماً استحساناً; لأنه عشر مهرها قبل الدخول – he gets fifty dirhams, which is half of the one-tenth (since before cons.).
The pattern mirrors full mahr cases but with proportional amounts.
5
Analyze cases where other property is named (cases 13-16)
When property other than mahr is named, after consummation (13-14) the husband gets that property; the mahr, if paid, remains with the wife, if unpaid, drops. Before consummation (15-16) the husband gets the named property; the mahr, if paid, stays with the wife without return; if unpaid, drops.
These cases have no qiyas/istihsan variation; the ruling follows the named condition.

Source Text

[رمز الحقائق: ٢٧٩/١]
Whether (the mahr is) received or not received, before or after consummation. And if he divorces his minor daughter by khul' with her property, it is not valid over her. (That is, the mahr.) Khul' of the minor. (That is, whether it is) by the woman (i.e., after consummation?) the father. Before or after consummation: this divides into aspects. Because either they name nothing, or they name the mahr or some of it, or other property. Each is two kinds: either before or after consummation, and the mahr is either received or not. Total sixteen aspects: First: they name nothing, khul' before consummation, mahr not received – each is free from the other's right regarding what was obligatory in the marriage according to the correct view. Second: they name nothing, before consummation, mahr received. Third: they name nothing, after consummation, mahr received. Fourth: like that, mahr not received. (He returns?) The ruling in these three is like the first. Fifth: they name the mahr, say one thousand dirhams. Sixth: after consummation and it is received – he takes all of it from her by condition. Seventh: before consummation and it is upon her (i.e., wife) with one thousand five hundred – one thousand by condition and five hundred by talaq before consummation by qiyas, and with the one thousand received only by istihsan. Eighth: the mahr is not received – all mahr is dropped from him and he takes the excess by qiyas, but does not take anything from her by istihsan. Ninth: they name part of the mahr, e.g., he divorces her by khul' for one-tenth of her mahr, and the mahr is one thousand, after consummation, mahr received – he takes one hundred dirhams from her, and the rest remains for her. Tenth: the mahr is not received – all mahr drops from him, one hundred by condition and the rest by khul'. Eleventh: before consummation, mahr received – he takes six hundred from her: one hundred as compensation for khul' and five hundred by talaq before consummation by qiyas, and he takes fifty dirhams by istihsan because it is one-tenth of her mahr before consummation. Twelfth: mahr not received – all drops from him. Thirteenth: they name property other than mahr, after consummation, mahr received – he gets the named, nothing else. Fourteenth: mahr not received – he gets the named, and the mahr drops from him by the ruling of khul'. Fifteenth: before consummation, mahr received – he gets the named by condition, and she keeps what she received, and she does not have to return anything from it. Sixteenth: it is not received – he gets the named by condition, and the mahr drops from him by khul'. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 279/1]
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of sixteen possible scenarios in a khul' transaction involving the mahr (dower). The author sets up the variables: whether compensation is named or not, the timing (before or after consummation), and whether the mahr has been paid (received) or not. Based on these, he lists sixteen cases. The first four cases are when no specific compensation is named; the ruling in these is that each spouse becomes free from the other's marriage-related rights (mahr is dropped if unpaid, and if paid, the wife keeps it? Actually careful: first case: before consummation, mahr not received – both free; second: before, mahr received – same; third: after, mahr received – same; fourth: after, mahr not received – same. So in all four when no compensation is named, the effect is mutual release. The fifth to eighth cases are when the full mahr is named as compensation. For example, if they name the entire mahr as compensation, after consummation and mahr received, the husband gets all of it back. Before consummation, if the mahr is one thousand and they name one thousand five hundred, then by qiyas (analogy) the husband gets the one thousand by condition and five hundred as the half mahr due upon talaq before consummation, but by istihsan (juristic preference), he only gets the one thousand received. Similarly in case eight, if mahr not received, by qiyas he gets all mahr plus excess, but by istihsan he gets nothing. The ninth to twelfth cases cover when part of the mahr is named. For example, naming one-tenth of the mahr: after consummation and mahr paid, he gets one-tenth back; if unpaid, the one-tenth is by condition and the rest drops. Thirteenth to sixteenth cases are when property other than mahr is named. In these, the named property is due, and the mahr is either retained by the wife (if received) or dropped (if unpaid) depending on timing. The commentary also references a ruling about the father divorcing his minor daughter by khul' with her property, which is covered in a later lesson. This enumeration demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of deriving rulings through both qiyas and istihsan, with istihsan often tempering the strict analogy.

Key Terms
قياساً (qiyāsan): By analogy; here used to extend the rule of talaq before consummation to khul'
استحساناً (istiḥsānan): By juristic preference; here used to modify the strict analogy to reach a more equitable result
المهر (al-mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife as part of the marriage contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In case seven (khul' before consummation, full mahr named as compensation, mahr received), what does the husband receive by istihsan?
A) The full named compensation plus half the mahr (1500 dirhams)
B) Only the named compensation (1000 dirhams)
C) Nothing
D) The full named compensation plus the entire mahr (2000 dirhams)

Key Takeaway
In khul', the Hanafi school prefers istihsan over strict qiyas to ensure the wife is not burdened beyond what she agreed to pay.

Father's Khul' of Minor Daughter: Talaq Valid, Compensation Invalid
Ruling on father's khul' of minor daughter with her property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a father performing khul' on behalf of his minor daughter using her property, and explain why the talaq occurs but the compensation is invalid.

Core Idea
A father's khul' of his minor daughter with her property is invalid for compensation, but the talaq still takes effect.

The father, as guardian, may not donate his daughter's property. When he performs khul' using her money, it is treated as a donation – invalid. However, the talaq (divorce) is not contingent on valid compensation; it falls as ba'in or raj'i per prior detail. This lesson unpacks the legal logic and distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
Recognize that the father performs khul' on behalf of his minor daughter using her property. Determine the two separate components: the talaq (dowry) and the compensation (mal). The text rules on validity of each.
2
Analyze the compensation (mal)
The khul' compensation is specified as the daughter's property. The text declares 'lam yajuz 'alayha' – not valid over her. Understand why: the father cannot donate her property. The husband does not deserve the specified amount, and the mahr is not dropped.
Contrast with khul' using valid property of the wife herself after majority.
3
Analyze the talaq
Despite invalid compensation, the talaq occurs. The text states 'wa tulliqat' (and she is divorced). The reasoning: talaq is not conditional on valid compensation; analogy to khul' with wine – talaq falls but no compensation due.
The type of talaq (ba'in or raj'i) follows earlier detail in the text (most correct view).
4
Distinguish the mother's case
The ruling is restricted to the father because he is guardian of property. If the mother performs khul' using her own property (or accepts a khul'), it is valid like a stranger. If she does not provide property and does not guarantee, talaq does not occur according to the correct view.
This highlights that the mother lacks authority over the daughter's property; her actions only bind herself.
5
Extract the underlying principle
The guardian cannot harm the minor's financial interest without benefit. Khul' using the minor's property is a gratuitous transfer (donation), which is not binding. The talaq is independent and valid because the effective cause (sabab) of talaq is the offer and acceptance, not the validity of compensation.
This principle applies broadly to other transactions on behalf of minors.

Source Text

(عيني، مستخلص، فتح)
And if he divorces his minor daughter by khul' with her property, it is not valid over her. (That is, the mahr.) Khul' of the minor. (That is, whether it is) by the woman (i.e., after consummation?) the father. Before or after consummation: this divides into aspects... [sixteen cases repeated] ...His minor daughter: that is, his young daughter from her husband. (T, 'A) 'It is not valid over her' means: if a man divorces his minor daughter by khul' with her property, the husband does not deserve what the father specified from the minor's property, and the mahr is not dropped, because khul' with her property is like a donation, so it is not binding on her. And his statement 'and she is divorced' means that talaq occurs as ba'in or raj'i according to the earlier detail in the most correct view, because the non-obligation of the compensation does not necessitate the non-occurrence of talaq. Do you not see that khul' with wine results in talaq occurring, yet nothing is obligatory? It is restricted to the father because if the mother performs khul' between her and the minor's husband with her own property or accepts, the khul' is complete like a stranger, and if she does not add and does not guarantee, the correct view is that talaq does not occur. ('Ayni, Mustakhlas, Fath)
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage deals specifically with the scenario where the father of a minor daughter (who is still married) performs a khul' on her behalf using her own property as compensation. The ruling is that such a khul' is not valid 'over her' meaning it is not binding on the minor daughter's property. The husband does not get the compensation specified by the father, and the mahr (dower) is not dropped; so the daughter retains her right to the mahr. However, the talaq (divorce) itself does occur, either as ba'in (irrevocable) or raj'i (revocable) according to the earlier detail in the most correct view. This is analogous to khul' with wine (which is not valid property): talaq still falls but no compensation is due. The restriction to the father is important: if the mother (who is not the guardian of property in this context) performs the khul' using her own property, or if she accepts a khul' offer, it is valid like a stranger's action. But if the mother does not add anything (i.e., does not provide her own property) and does not guarantee the compensation, the correct view is that talaq does not occur. The distinction is because the father has guardianship over the minor's property, but he cannot donate her property; khul' with her property is considered a donation and thus invalid. The talaq falls because it is not conditional upon the validity of the compensation.

Key Terms
صغيرة (ṣaghīra): A minor daughter who is under guardianship of her father for property matters.
لم يجز عليها (lam yajuz 'alayhā): Not valid/effective over her; specifically, the khul' compensation is not binding on the minor's property.
التبرع (al-tabarru'): Donation; a gratuitous transfer of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A father cannot use his minor daughter's property for khul' compensation, but the divorce still takes effect because talaq is not dependent on valid compensation.

Definition of al-'Unayn and Ruling for the Majbub Husband
Defines the impotent man and states immediate separation for the castrated husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the 'unayn and the majbub, and state the Hanafi ruling for each: immediate separation for the majbub and one-year deferment for the 'unayn.

Core Idea
Majbub: immediate separation; 'Unayn: one-year deferment.

The chapter on impotence (bāb al-'unan) distinguishes two types of husbands with separate rulings. The castrated husband (majbub) is hopeless, so separation is immediate. The impotent husband ('unayn) may be curable, so a deferment of one year is granted. Let's parse the definitions and their legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Recognize al-'unayn (impotent) and al-majbub (castrated) as distinct categories in the passage.
'Unayn and khasi also share the deferment ruling.
2
Understand the definition of 'unayn
'Unayn is defined as one who cannot reach women at all, or can only reach a thayyib (non-virgin) but not a virgin.
The definition focuses on permanent inability.
3
Note the ruling for majbub
If a woman finds her husband is majbub, the qadi separates them immediately (farraqa fi al-hal).
No deferment because the defect is incurable.
4
Note the ruling for 'unayn/khasi
If the husband is 'unayn or khasi, a one-year deferment is granted (ajala sanah).
Deferment allows time for potential cure.
5
Distinguish the underlying reason
The key difference is hope of cure: majbub is hopeless; 'unayn/khasi may potentially be cured.
This illa (effective cause) determines the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العنين هو من لا يصل إلى النساء، أو يصل إلى الثيب دون الأبكار، وجدت زوجها مجبوبا فرق في الحال وأجل سنة لو عنيناً، أو خصياً.
Chapter of the impotent man. He is the one who cannot reach women, or reaches the non-virgin but not the virgins. His wife found her husband castrated: they separate immediately. And a year is deferred if he is impotent or castrated (khasi).
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It defines 'unayn (impotent man) as one who cannot have intercourse with women at all due to a permanent defect, or can only have intercourse with a previously married woman (thayyib) but not a virgin. Then it gives two distinct rulings: (1) If a woman finds her husband is majbub (castrated, with penis and testicles removed), the qadi separates them immediately without any deferment, because there is no hope of cure. (2) If the husband is 'unayn (impotent) or khasi (one whose testicles are removed but penis remains), the qadi grants a deferral of one year to see if he can perform. The separation in the first case is immediate; in the second, it is conditional upon non-performance within the year.

Key Terms
العنين (al-'unayn): A man who cannot have intercourse with women due to a permanent defect, or can only have intercourse with a thayyib but not a virgin.
المجبوب (al-majbub): A man whose penis and testicles are completely cut off (castrated).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a woman who finds her husband is majbub (castrated) according to the Hanafi school?
A) She must wait one year for him to be cured.
B) The qadi separates them immediately without any deferment.
C) She must go to court and request a divorce.
D) The marriage is automatically annulled.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a castrated husband (majbub) warrants immediate separation, while an impotent husband ('unayn) receives a one-year deferment.

One-Year Deferment for the Impotent Husband: Proof and Rationale
Explains the one-year deferment for 'unayn/khasi with evidence and opposing view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling of the one-year deferment for the 'unayn or khasi, identify its proof from the Companions' practice, and distinguish between the lunar and solar year opinions.

Core Idea
A one-year deferment reveals whether impotence is curable; the Companions' consensus overrides an isolated hadith.

When a wife discovers her husband is impotent, she has a right to seek separation. But before separating, the Hanafi school requires a one-year deferment to determine if the condition is temporary or permanent. This deferment, established by the practice of Caliphs Umar, Ali, and Ibn Mas'ud, allows the body to respond to seasonal changes. In this lesson, we explore the proof, the rationale of the four seasons, and the intra-madhhab difference over whether the year is lunar or solar.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its scope
What is the ruling? Who does it apply to? The text rules that a year is deferred (wajib) if the husband is either 'unayn (impotent) or khasi (castrated).
The ruling covers both total impotence and testicle removal.
2
Identify the primary proof
The proof is the consensus (ijma) of the Companions Umar, Ali, and Ibn Mas'ud, who practiced the deferment. In Hanafi usul, the practice of the Companions when not contradicted is a binding proof.
The text says 'hākadhā nuqila 'an 'Umar wa 'Ali wa Ibn Mas'ūd'.
3
Analyze the rational reasoning (ta'lil)
The wife has a right to intercourse, but the husband's inability may be due to a temporary defect. A year is set because it covers all four seasons (moisture, dryness, heat, cold), hoping one season suits his temperament and cures him.
This is a rational (qiyas-based) justification for the one-year period.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference
The default is the lunar year (354 days + fractions). However, from Imam Abu Hanifa, the solar year (365 days) is mentioned out of caution. The relied-upon (muftā bihi) position is the lunar year.
The text says 'wa 'an al-Imām tu'tabaru al-sanat al-shamsiyya ihtiyātan'.
5
Respond to the opposing view
The Zāhiriyya argue no deferment, citing the hadith of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Zubayr's wife. The Hanafi response: the Companions' consensus is stronger and overrides that hadith.
This illustrates how strengthening a proof through consensus (ijmā') takes precedence over an isolated (āḥād) report.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجل سنة لو عنيناً، أو خصياً؛ هكذا نقل عن عمر وعلي وابن مسعود له؛ ولأن الحق ثابت لها في الوطء، ويحتمل أن يكون الامتناع لعلة معترضة ويحتمل أن يكون لآفة أصلية، فلابد من رفة ذلك، فقدر بالسنة؛ لاشتمالها على الفصول الأربعة المشتملة على الرطوبة واليبوسة والحرارة والبرودة، فعسى أن يوافق فصل منها طبعه فيزول ما به العلة باعتدال الطبع، وتعتبر السنة القمرية وهي ثلاث مائة وأربعة وخمسون يوماً وخمس يوم وسدسه، وعن الإمام تعتبر السنة الشمسية احتياطاً، وقالت الظاهرية لا يؤجل ولا يفرق؛ لحديث امرأة عبد الرحمن بن الزبير، فإنه عليها لم يؤجله حين شكت إليه. ولنا: إجماع الصحابة له على تأجيله.
And a year is deferred if he is impotent or castrated. This is transmitted from Umar, Ali, and Ibn Mas'ud. Because the right to intercourse is established for her, and it is possible that the refusal is due to a temporary cause or an original defect, so clarification is needed. It is set to one year because it includes the four seasons of moisture, dryness, heat, and cold, so perhaps one season will suit his temperament and the defect will go away. The lunar year is considered: 354 days and 1/5 and 1/6. From the Imam (Abu Hanifa), the solar year is considered out of caution. The Zahiriyya said: no deferment and no separation, based on the hadith of the wife of Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Zubayr, for the Prophet (SAW) did not defer for her when she complained. Our proof: the consensus of the Companions on deferring for him.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling and proof for the one-year deferment. It begins with the ruling: a year is deferred for the 'unayn or khasi. The proof is twofold: (1) the practice of the Companions Umar, Ali, and Ibn Mas'ud, who granted a year deferment; (2) rational reasoning: the wife has a right to intercourse, but the husband's inability may be due to a temporary illness or a permanent defect. To determine this, a year is set because it covers all four seasons, and a change in temperament might cure him. The year considered is the lunar year (354 days + fractions), but Imam Abu Hanifa reportedly considered the solar year out of caution (365 days). The Zahiriyya (Zahirite school) opposed, citing the hadith of Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Zubayr's wife, where the Prophet did not defer. The Hanafi response is that the Companions' consensus overrides that isolated hadith.

Key Terms
الخصي (al-khasi): A man whose testicles are removed but penis remains.
العنين (al-'unayn): A man who is impotent and cannot achieve or maintain an erection for intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the Hanafis set the deferment for the 'unayn to one year?
A) Because the Prophet (SAW) specifically ordered it.
B) Because the Companions Umar, Ali, and Ibn Mas'ud practiced it and because the four seasons may affect health.
C) Because one year is the time for a woman to remarry.
D) Because it matches the iddah period.

Key Takeaway
The one-year deferment for the impotent husband is wajib under Hanafi law, grounded in the Companions' consensus and rational reasoning of seasonal cure, with the lunar year being the operative standard for fatwa.

Consequences of Copulation or Failure Within the Year
Ruling on copulation and nature of separation if no intercourse occurs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the deferment year for an 'unayn, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and distinguish between ba'in talaq and faskh in the inter-madhhab disagreement.

Core Idea
If the husband fails to consummate within the year, the qadi issues a ba'in talaq; it is not an annulment.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a critical issue in marriage law: what happens when an 'unayn (impotent) husband fails to consummate within the one-year deferment granted by the qadi. The ruling involves both an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the qadi's intervention is necessary and an inter-madhhab dispute on whether the resulting separation is a divorce (talaq) or an annulment (faskh). Understanding these layers is essential for grasping the Hanafi approach to marital defects and the role of the judiciary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the effect of intercourse during the year
Examine the first sentence: If the husband has intercourse even once within the year, the deferment is void. This means the wife loses the right to demand separation based on that deferment.
The phrase 'ولو مرة واحدة' emphasizes that even a single act of intercourse nullifies the option.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute on the separation procedure
Compare the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Abu Hanifa requires the qadi to order the husband to divorce; if he refuses, the qadi issues a ba'in talaq. The Sahibayn say the wife becomes separated automatically at the end of the year.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view, as indicated by the structure of the matn.
3
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute on the nature of the separation
Contrast the Hanafi position (ba'in talaq) with the Shafi'i/Hanbali position (faskh). Identify the key argument: the Hanafis attribute the separation to the husband via the qadi's deputyship, while the opposing school attributes it to the wife's initiative.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is that ba'in talaq counts as one of the three divorces, whereas faskh does not.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi proof
Follow the reasoning: The husband is obligated to retain his wife in kindness; when that is impossible, release becomes wajib. Since the husband refuses, the qadi acts as his deputy, making the divorce attributable to him. A binding marriage does not admit annulment.
The proof relies on the concept of niyaba (deputyship) and the principle that a valid nikah is not subject to faskh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن وطئ ولو مرة واحدة في السنة فيها وبطل التأجيل. وإلا بانت بتفريق القاضي عند امتناع الزوج عن تطليقها، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأنه لما تعذر عليه الإمساك بالمعروف وجب عليه التسريح بالإحسان، فإن فعل الزوج وطلق فيها، وإلا ناب القاضي منابه دفعاً للظلم عنها، وعندهما وهو رواية عنه: بانت بغير تفريق؛ لأن الشرع خيرها عند تمام الحول. ثم الفرقة طلقة بائنة عندنا، وعند الشافعي وأحمد هي فسخ؛ لأنه فرقة من جهتها، ولنا أن هذه الفرقة من جهته؛ لأن الواجب عليه الإمساك، فإذا فات وجب التسريح، فلما امتنع هو قام القاضي مقامه، فكان الفعل منسوباً إليه فكان طلاقاً بائناً ليتحقق دفع الظلم عنها، والنكاح الصحيح النافذ اللازم لا يحتمل الفسخ.
If he has intercourse, even once within the year, then the deferment is void. Otherwise (if he does not), she becomes separate by the qadi's separation when the husband refuses to divorce her. This is according to Abu Hanifa: since he is unable to retain her in kindness, it is obligatory to release her with goodness. If the husband does so and divorces her, fine; otherwise the qadi acts in his place to remove harm. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and a narration from him (Abu Hanifa): she becomes separated without qadi's intervention because the law gave her the choice at the end of the year. Then the separation is a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce according to us. According to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, it is a faskh (annulment) because it is a separation from her side. Our proof: this separation is from his side because the duty on him is retention; when that is impossible, release is obligatory. When he refused, the qadi stood in his place, so the act is attributed to him, thus it is a ba'in talaq to remove harm. A valid, effective, and binding marriage does not allow annulment.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage covers two issues: First, if the husband has intercourse even once during the deferment year, the deferment ends and the wife no longer has the option to seek separation. Second, if he fails to copulate, separation occurs. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa says the qadi must order a divorce; if the husband refuses, the qadi divorces on his behalf. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) say she becomes separated automatically at the end of the year without qadi intervention, because the law gave her the choice. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's. Then there is an inter-madhhab dispute: The Hanafis consider this separation a ba'in talaq (an irrevocable divorce from the husband's side), while al-Shafi'i and Ahmad consider it a faskh (annulment of the marriage contract). The Hanafi reasoning is that the duty of retention (imsaak) has failed, so release (tasreeh) becomes wajib. Since the husband refuses, the qadi acts as his deputy, so the divorce is attributed to the husband, making it a talaq, not a faskh. Marriage in Hanafi is a binding contract that cannot be annulled without cause; the husband's impotence is not a ground for faskh but for separation through talaq.

Key Terms
الفرقة طلقة بائنة (al-furqa talqatan ba'inatan): A separation that counts as one irrevocable divorce (ba'in sughra), meaning the husband can remarry her with a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens if the 'unayn husband does not have intercourse within the deferment year?
A) The marriage is automatically annulled.
B) The wife becomes separated without any action.
C) The qadi gives her a ba'in talaq if the husband refuses to divorce.
D) The husband must pay compensation.

Key Takeaway
For an 'unayn husband, failure to consummate within the year leads to a ba'in talaq via the qadi; the separation is a divorce, not an annulment.

Dispute When Husband Claims Copulation and Wife Denies
Ruling on proof of copulation when husband claims and wife denies, based on her virginity status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi ruling when a husband claims intercourse and the wife denies, and EXPLAIN why the ruling differs for a virgin vs. a thayyib.

Core Idea
For a thayyib, the husband's oath is accepted; for a virgin, her physical state is conclusive.

In marriage disputes, evidence can be conflicting. This passage addresses a specific scenario: after a year without consummation, the husband claims he had intercourse, but the wife denies. The resolution hinges on whether she is a virgin or not. We will see why the Hanafi school gives weight to the husband's oath for a thayyib but relies on physical examination for a virgin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
What triggers the dispute: the husband, after the one-year deferment, claims he has had intercourse. The wife denies.
This follows from the earlier rule of khayr al-bikr (option for a virgin wife after one year).
2
Classify the wife's status
Is she a virgin (bikr) or a thayyib (previously married)? The outcome depends on this classification.
Virginity is determined by examination of trustworthy women (qabila).
3
Apply the ruling for each status
If virgin and confirmed, the wife is given a choice to stay or separate. If thayyib, the husband's word is accepted with an oath, and she loses her right to separation.
The choice is immediate in the same sitting.
4
Analyse the legal reasoning (talil)
Why the difference? Virginity necessarily negates penetration (bikr cannot be penetrated without breaking the hymen). For a thayyib, no such evidence; the husband is 'denying in meaning' her right to separation, and the legal consideration is for meaning, not form.
The principle: al-ibrah li al-ma'na la li al-surah.
5
Note the underlying principle
The Hanafi rule that 'consideration is for meaning, not form' is applied here. The husband's claim is not seen as an admission but as a defense against separation.
Analogous to the depositor claiming return of deposit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو قال: وطئت وأنكرت، نظر إليها النساء، فإن قلن هي بكر خيرت المرأة في الحال بين الإقامة والفرقة في مجلسها، وإن كانت ثيباً صدق بحلفه؛ لأنه منكر معنى لثبوت حقها بالفرقة، وإن منكرة للوصول صورة؛ إذ العبرة للمعنى لا للصورة. بخلاف البكارة؛ لأن ثبوتها ينفي الوصول إليها ضرورة فتخير بقولهن.
If he (the husband after the year) says: 'I have had intercourse,' and she denies, then women look at her. If they say she is a virgin, the woman is given the choice in that sitting between staying and separating. If she is a thayyib (non-virgin) originally, his word is accepted with his oath because he is denying in meaning her right to separation; she is claiming in form that he did not reach her. The consideration is for meaning, not form. Unlike virginity, because its establishment necessarily negates reaching, so she is given the choice based on their (the women's) statement.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage addresses the procedural dispute when the husband claims he has had intercourse during the year, and the wife denies. The resolution depends on whether the wife is originally a virgin or a thayyib. If the wife is originally a virgin, and trustworthy women (qabila) examine her and affirm that she is still a virgin (i.e., no intercourse occurred), then the wife is given the option in the same sitting to either stay in the marriage or seek separation. This is because virginity necessarily negates penetration. If the wife is originally a thayyib (non-virgin), her denial does not automatically prove lack of intercourse, because a thayyib could have been penetrated without clear signs. Therefore, the husband's claim is given credence: he takes an oath that he did have intercourse, and the wife's right to demand separation is lost. The reasoning: the husband is denying the wife's right to separation in meaning (ma'nan), even though in form (suratan) he is affirming he copulated. The legal consideration (ibra) is given to the meaning, not the form. This is similar to the case of a depositor who claims he returned the deposit: the trustee's word is taken with oath because of the meaning.

Key Terms
ثيب (thayyib): A woman who is not a virgin, having been previously married or for any other reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the husband claims he had intercourse within the deferment year and the wife denies, what happens if the wife is a virgin (bikr)?
A) The husband is believed with an oath.
B) The wife is believed without evidence.
C) Women examine her; if they confirm virginity, the wife is given the choice.
D) The qadi decides based on the husband's character.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband claims intercourse and wife denies, the wife's virginity is decisive: if she is a virgin and confirmed, she has a choice; if she is thayyib, the husband's oath prevails.

Iddah of Fasid Nikah, Shubha, and Umm Walad
Rulings on iddah for invalid marriage, mistaken intercourse, and slave mother

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the iddah for fasid nikah, shubha intercourse, and umm walad, and explain why it is three cycles by applying the analogy to marriage (zawal al-firash) and distinguishing it from istibra'.

Core Idea
Iddah for invalid marriage, shubha, and umm walad is three cycles because the bond is like marriage, not mere ownership.

This lesson examines the Hanafi view on the waiting period for three distinct situations: an invalid marriage, intercourse under a mistaken belief, and the death of a master of a slave mother (umm walad). While some schools treat these cases differently, the Hanafi school consistently applies a full iddah of three menstrual cycles based on the principle of removal of the marital bed (zawal al-firash). Understanding this ruling clarifies the strength of the bond in these relationships and the rationale behind the full waiting period.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling for each category: fasid nikah, shubha, and umm walad. Note the condition of not being pregnant or menopausal.
The ruling is three menstrual cycles or three months if not menstruating.
2
Identify the opponent's view
Recognize that Shafi'i and Malik hold that the iddah of umm walad is one cycle (istibra'), and al-Awza'i holds four months ten days.
The opposition is based on different analogies: one to mere ownership, another to widowhood.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i/Maliki argument: since ownership of the right hand ends, it resembles the istibra' of a purchased slave (one cycle). Also note al-Awza'i's reliance on a hadith from Amr ibn al-As.
The Hanafis consider the hadith weak and the analogy to ownership inadequate.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi reasoning: the bond of umm walad is stronger than mere ownership; it is a form of marriage (firash). Hence the iddah resembles the iddah of a married woman. They cite the practice of Umar, Ali, and Ibn Mas'ud.
The key is the concept of 'zawal al-firash' (removal of the bed) which triggers a full iddah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Compare the practical outcomes: under Hanafi, the woman waits three cycles; under Shafi'i/Maliki, one cycle; under Awza'i, four months ten days.
The difference directly affects when the woman can remarry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنكوحة نكاحاً فاسداً: أي عدة المنكوحة بالنكاح الفاسد، وعدة الموطوءة بشبهة، وعدة أم الولد بعد وفاة سيدها بشرط أن لا يكن حاملات ولا آيسات الحيض؛ لأن عدتهن للتعرف عن براءة الرحم لانقضاء حق النكاح، والحيض هو المعرف في غير الحامل والآيسة، فلا يختلف بين الموت وغيره، فإن قلت: فعلى هذا ينبغي أن يكتفى بحيضة كما في الاستبراء؛ لانه يحصل بها التعريف قلت : النكاح الفاسد ملحق بالصحيح كما في البيع، حتى يفيد الملك إذا اتصل به القبض، والوطء بشبهة هو كالنكاح الفاسد حتى يجب به المهر، وعدة أم الولد وجب بزوال الفراش فأشبهت عدة النكاح، وقال الشافعي ومالك حمل لانها تحب العدة على أم الولد بحيضة واحدة؛ لأنها تحب بزوال ملك اليمين فشابهت الاستبراء، يروى ذلك عن عائشة ما وابن عمر ما، وقال الأوزاعي: في موت مولاها أربعة أشهر وعشر، رواه عمرو بن العاص عن رسول الله لا أخرجه أبو داود وضعفه. ولنا أن العدة وجبت بزوال الفراش فأشبه عدة النكاح، وإمامنا في ذلك عمر بن الخطاب له فإنه قال: عدة أم الولد ثلاث حيض ولو كانت ممن لا تحيض فعدتها ثلاثة أشهر كما في النكاح، وهو قول علي وابن مسعود ما - عدتها
And the one married with a fasid marriage: i.e., the iddah of the woman married with an invalid marriage, and the iddah of a woman with whom intercourse occurred through shubha (mistaken belief), and the iddah of umm walad after her master's death, provided they are not pregnant nor menopausal. Because their iddah is to ascertain the purity of the womb after the end of the marriage right, and menstruation is the identifier in non-pregnant and non-menopausal women, so it does not differ between death and other. If you say: on this basis, one menstruation should suffice as in istibra', because identification is achieved by it. I say: the invalid marriage is attached to the valid one, as in sale, so it confers ownership when delivery is connected. And intercourse by shubha is like an invalid marriage, so mahr is obligatory. And the iddah of umm walad became obligatory due to the removal of the bed, so it resembles the iddah of marriage. Shafi'i and Malik said: the iddah of umm walad is one menstruation because it becomes obligatory due to the end of ownership of the right hand, so it resembles istibra'. This is narrated from Aisha and Ibn Umar. Al-Awza'i said: upon her master's death, four months and ten days, narrated by Amr ibn al-As from the Messenger of Allah, which Abu Dawood reported and weakened. Our proof is that the iddah became obligatory due to the removal of the bed, so it resembles the iddah of marriage. And our imam in this is Umar ibn al-Khattab, who said: the iddah of umm walad is three menstrual cycles, and if she is not menstruating, three months, as in marriage. This is the saying of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with them).
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage provides rulings on the iddah for three categories: a woman married under an invalid contract (fasid nikah), a woman who was cohabited with under a mistaken belief (shubha), and a slave mother of a child (umm walad) after her master's death. In all three cases, the Hanafi ruling is that her iddah is three menstrual cycles (or three months if not menstruating), the same as for a valid marriage. The reasoning is that these situations involve the end of a marital bond or bed, so the iddah serves to determine the purity of the womb. The author responds to potential objections: why not just one cycle like istibra' (waiting period for slave women)? Because here the relationship is more akin to marriage, so the full iddah applies. The passage then contrasts with other schools: Shafi'i and Malik hold umm walad's iddah is one cycle (like istibra'), and al-Awza'i holds four months ten days. The Hanafi proof is based on the practice of Umar, Ali, and Ibn Mas'ud. The Hanafi school follows the analogy to marriage (zawal al-firash).

Key Terms
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master; upon master's death she becomes free.
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after the dissolution of a marriage or bond, to ensure the womb is free from pregnancy.
الفراش (al-firash): The marital bed or the bond that establishes a relationship analogous to marriage.
الاستبراء (al-istibra'): The waiting period for a slave woman after change of ownership, usually one menstrual cycle, to ensure no pregnancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the iddah of a woman in a fasid nikah who is not pregnant and regularly menstruates?
A) One menstrual cycle
B) Three menstrual cycles
C) Four months and ten days
D) No iddah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the iddah for fasid nikah, shubha, and umm walad is three cycles (or three months) because the bond is analogous to marriage, not mere ownership.
